“Certainly no such distinctive verse has appeared
among us for years.”—Sydney Morning Herald.
“ A collection of really admirable poems. There is no
mistaking the swing of his verses. Small wonder that
that suclt lines as these should stir the hearts of men. .
We might go on quoting indefinitely.”—The Queens’-
Landkr.
“A welcome addition to the scanty roll of writers of
distinctively Australian verse. The writer knows how to
tell a stirring incident in a spirited and vigorous manner,
and the local coloring is exact and truthful. He is evi-
dently quite familiar with every aspect of bush and
station life.”—Ausrralasian.
“The body of what lies between these covers is in some
respects distinctly better, as Australian literature, than
the few of Gordon’s poems in which the local element has
been made unmistakeahle.”—Sydney Daily Telegraph.
“The dominant tone is that of an intense affection and
longing for the wild, free, natural life of the country.”—
Town and Country Journal.
“I tell you, friends, this hook of ‘ Banjo’s’ is the best
thing I have seen done in Australia. It is pathetic,
humorous,strong, descriptive, and—Australian!”—Stock
and Station Journal.
“The volume teems with vivid pictures , . . and
all is told in vigorous verse, having a splendid swing and
freedom.”—Maitland Mercury.
“In my opinion this collection comprises the best bush
ballads written since the death of Lindsay Gordon.”—
“ Roi. 7 Boldrewood.”

To Correspondent.—J.R.W. : It may take
months or years to get MSS. past a London pub-
lisher’s reader. Nine-tenths of the stuff received
is never read ; unless it is well introduced, or
attracts on a cursory glance, it is pitched aside'in
favour of established names and known contents.
If you worry, you may get the stuff back : are you
quite sure it deserves to be kept?
MOTE.—As most of the books men-
tioned are in strong - demand, some may
be temporarily out of stock. In such
case, orders will be filed, and the books
forwarded immediately on arrival of next
shipment.
Any book or periodical will be for-
warded on receipt of price, particulars
of which will be given to enquirers. A
list of periodicals may be had on appli-
cation.

Those who have taken this medicine are amazed

it is invaluable, as it effects a complete cure. It
is most comforting in allaying tickling and irrita-
tion in the throat, and giving strength to the voice,
and it never allows a cough or asthma to become
chronic. No house should be without it, as, taken
at the beginning, a dose is generally sufficient,
and a complete cure is certain.

g L I N D S.
The word is suggestive. Many dealers keep their
customers very much in the dark as to the true
state of things. So to speak, they get behind their
own blinds because the circumstances won’t allow
them to emerge into the full light.
OUR REPRESENTATIVE IN CHINA
writes as follows
I should like to mentnai certain matters in connection
with the blind trade. With every order I place I INSIST
ON INSPECTION before packing, with the result that
not less than 26 to 80 per cent, are rejected. But I And
this season a firm has placed large orders, but ABSO-
LUTELY NO INSPECTION takes plaoe, consequently
makers are competing for the orders at lower rates on
these terms.
We take our friends behind the scenes so that
they may see WHAT A TRAGEDY the blind
trade is, and so that they may, when buying blinds,
discriminate between honest dealers and the other
sort. No doubt the blinds WHICH ARE
SHIPPED WITHOUT INSPECTION are
offered at lower prices than SELECTED BLINDS,
but wise people will prefer giving a trifle more,
should it be necessary, with the certainty of get-
ting BLINDS which offer some show of protec-
tion, and that do not tumble in pieces after a
fortnight’s wear.
BAMBOO BLINDS
made of the outside of skin or the bamboo in
its natural color. SPECIALLY SELECTED.
Fitted with CORDS and pulleys ready to put up.
It may be added that the CORDS are strong and
suitable, and not liable to rot and break on the
least provocation. Sizes from 3ft. wide by 6ft.
deep to 12ft. wide by Bft. deep ; prices, Is. to
ss. 6d. each blind.
CHINESE BLINDS
made of fine split bamboo, painted green on both
sides, and fitted with CORDS and pulleys of
best quality. These blinds are SPECIALLY
SELECTED. Sizes from 3ft. wide by 6ft. deep
to 12ft. wide by 10ft. deep ; prices from Is. Bd. to
7s. 6d. each blind.
BEAD AND REED BLINDS
Charming assortment of colors and styles. These
blinds are available for halls, doorways and win-
dows, They say they frighten the flies away. If
so, they are worth a pound an inch. Prices from
2s. 9d. to 15s.

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The blood of Woman weak is on thy head,
The blood of Mother wronged, yet held accursed,
Thy head, O, Butcher-People, bloody red,
At paps of pale hypocrisy wet-nursed.
Her blood cries out to all the firmament
From shambles of a fierce Executive ;
The lees of lust may wallow in content,
Yet never learn to spell the word—Forgive.
For mercy cringing for its own pet sins,
Lust clutches helpless debtors by the throat,
Yet, “ Blood yells blood ! ’’ and ends as it begins,
Demanding payment to the final groat.
Nor meets a farthing of its own just debts,
Yet gloats on gallows and “ a sick’ning thud ” ;
With feelings Pharisaical, forgets
Its share in spilt infantieidal blood.
A maiden—nay, a child—of scarce fourteen,
Deflowered, ere yet her nature’s innocence
Can grasp the problem of the might-have-been !
Woe weights a motherhood past penitence.
See Mother Grundy’s skirts draw back at that!
See Christian sympathy grow coldly dull !
She, knapsacked with a noxious bastard brat,
Must eafn its suck as may a pavement trull.
Her ravisher may flaunt it with the Great!
May preach State scholars moral platitudes,
May “ smell his hat ” in kirk, hand round “the
plate,’’
And “lead in prayer ” in pious interludes ;
May visit “ Gu vmentouse,” “the mould of form,”
And smile on Bosomed Fashion as a boon.
She, overwhelmed by Icy Virtue’s storm,
Is euspidored—“ Sassiety’s ” spittoon !
Now rages battle’s tumult in her breast,
A fight so fierce it cannot long endure,
A war between the Mother, babe caressed,
And Woman, striving still to flee the sewer.
; The end was certain when the fight began ;
; Hell’s legions held a glad corroboree ;
( They knew that Icy Virtue’s moral plan
Swift murders Marriagelesss Maternity.
Next, see the Mother pleading with her kind
To lift the Knapsack off her weary back ;
Still see religious elements combined
To force her staggering along the track.
No hand of help ! A trull ! A bastard brat!
The grave alone gives rest, nor death too soon.
The grave ? There is deliverance in that !
See how inviting is the still lagoon.
A moment’s madness and a Murder done !
As simply done as Murder was before
When lechery the Child-Girl’s virtue won—
Choked out a life it never could restore.
Yet, when Humanity appealed for grace.
The Apoplectic Powers took no reck ;
“Thumbs down ” for blood ! —so judged, ne’er be
their case !
i They took Man’s Victim and they broke her
neck.
More merciful was She, in her wild deed
To her unwedlocked babe, than others showed,
Who called to power the Furies that decreed
Her life a forfeit to a Bloody Code.
The hour of death excels the hour of birth,
And bastardy ferments with bitter leaven;
At least She saved It from “ a hell on earth,”
And who dare say the pair are not in Heaven ?
Whose was,the Murder? Whose the Murders
twain ?
Spirit of Justice! name us. whose the guilt?
By cause of whom that baby bastard slain?
By whom was that Girl-Mother’s life-blood
spilt ?
Spirit of Cain ! rejoice thou in our Law,
Proclaim the shameless, bloody truth on oath—
The oath of Cam ! —the bloody truth and raw !
The murderers of one did murder both !
Thus Icy Virtue weds with bloody Cain—
That land is surely great and pure and free
Whose only Foundling Hospital a fane
In Murder’s Gave, hard-by the Gallows Tree.
c.

A deputation of Salvationists waited on
the War-Minister at Hobart praying that a blast
of puns be tired in honor of the landing of
General Booth. The petition was refused, and the
deputation went outside and wept on the pave-
ment.
Whenever the Age wishes to be particu-
larly impressive in criminological subjects it
quotes “ the eminent writer, Dr. Garth Wilkin-
son.” The mystery is, however, that Southern
students of criminology have sought in vain for
some trace of the weighty Wilkinson’s works
which appear to be unknown in Europe and
America.

Ex-Vic. Premier Patterson, who re-
cently died, was really a victim to the Brassey
razzle-dazzle, and the grand national grovel
of reception-day. The ex-Premier had la grippe,
and had been advised to nurse himself, but
refused to be absent from the tremendous cere-
monial down the bay, and the swearing-in func-
tion, because he ‘‘felt it was his duty ’’to help
idl the gubernatorial eye. He returned from the
affair of state considerably the worse, and died a
martyr to Imperialism.

IMPORTANT NOTICE TO CIGARETTE SMOKERS.
WE beg to inform our customers and cigarette smokers generally that the American Tobacco Co.,
Ltd., of N.S.W., who control the sale of cigarettes known under the brands of “ Allen and
Ginter’s Straight-cut No. 1,” “Cameo” and “ Old Judge,” and who manufacture the colonial-made
cigarettes called “ Pure Gold ” and “Dixon’s No. 1,” have given us official notice that they will not
supply us with any of their American-made cigarettes unless we agree to relinquish the agency held by
us for New South Wales for the world-renowned English firm of W. D. and H. O. WILLS, Limited,
the manufacturers of those popular high-class cigarettes knoym as
“ THREE OJLSTLIBS 77 and “ COIF’S ZLA-ICT. 75
We have declined to conform with this request, as we feel it would not be to the nterest of
cigarette smokers in New South Wales to allow the American Tobacco Co., Limited, to monopolise
the whole of the cigarette trade.. We, therefore, appeal to cigarette smokers to try the celebrated
Mills’ “Three Canties” and “ Capstan " Cigarettes,
And compare their quality with that of any other. We are confident that their verdict will be in
favor of the
“Three Castles” and “Capstan” Cigarettes,
And by smoking these they will prevent any one Company from attempting to dictate what people
should or should not smoke.

SATURDAY, NOVEMBER 16, 1895.
A British. Syndicate.
A few days ago it was cabled from England
that a chartered co. was in process of forma-
tion, under the auspices of Colonial Secretary
Chamberlain, with a view to taking over
the Northern Territory of Australia and
working it as a purely British speculation.
It says a lot for the blank indifference of the
Australian people upon great national ques-
tions that this stupendous announcement
appears so far to have excited no public
interest whatever. That small item of intel-
ligence told of, perhaps, the greatest danger
that has ever yet threatened Australia.
Nevertheless, Australia in general, being
busy over its great annual horse-carnival, and
over the Brassby reception and other show
items, has apparently not even inquired
about its authenticity.
* * *
The Northern Territory comprises one-
sixth part of this continent. Though not
yet thoroughly explored, it is well known
to be a rich country—rich in gold, silver,
copper, lead, coal and iron, and in pastoral
and agricultural land. So far, it remains
almost void of settlement, solely on account
of its inaccessibility. The railway to connect
it with Adelaide has not yet reached its
southernmost border ; the two great Queens-
land lines, which will ultimately connect
with it, are still many miles away from its
eastern frontier ; and Westralia hasn’t even
begun to make a railway towards it from the
west. Also, its principal seaport is an
immense distance by sea from anywhere.
It has been “temporarily ceded” to South
Australia, and all that has been done for it
has been at the expense of that province.
S.A. built its solitary railway line, 150 miles
in length, constructed its mads and tele-
graphs, supplied it with public schools, mail
services, police protection, law courts, par-
liamentary representation, and other alleged
adjuncts to civilisation, and has made up its
revenue deficiencies lo the amount of nearly
a million. Britain neither explored
the Territory, nor occupied it, nor
spent anything upon it, nor did any-
thing whatever to promote its ad-
vancement. Nevertheless, it remains on
paper a British possession, and Britain has
still a paper-right to assume the direct
ownership of it. No attempt was made
to exercise this right in the days when
the Territory was practically useless on
account of its utter isolation from the
outer world. But now that railways are
slowly approaching its borders, and
population is beginning to appear in the
adjacent regions of Westralia, and there is
•some prospect that the Territory will, before
very long, become a possession of colossal
value, the British claims seem to have sud-
denly revived, and the above-mentioned
syndicate is heard of for the first time.
• • •
The report may not be wholly true, but
even the rumor of such an aggression should
have stirred Australia to its foundations.
If the Territory is handed over, the first,
last and only object of the syndicate will be
to make money out of its possession. The
Government will consist of a board of direc-
tors in London. As the board will not live
in the country and will share none of its ills,
it will have no object whatever except to
establish and then grind down a servile
population, and make money. Even the
Boodlbwraith Ministry of Queensland and
the unspeakably bad Government of Wes-
tralia are restrained to some extent by the
fact that they live in the country, and, con-
sequently, don’t want to make it wholly un-
fit for a white man s occupation. Also, they
are restrained by the popular vote which has
to be reckoned with, no matter how the
electoral rolls may be manipulated. To the
syndicate it won’t matter what kind of a hell
the Territory may become, for it will be
16,000 miles away. It won’t be restrained
by popular suffrage, for there will be no
suffrage or Parliament or popular representa-
tion whatever. The syndicate will prac-
tically supply the law-courts, the police, the
executive, the Governor, the military
forces, and all the rest. It will be the
universal landlord and the beginning and
end of everything. Under these conditions
what kind of a Government it may supply
can be imagined, and there is practically no
kind of a Government which it may not
supply.
♦
The Territory, under syndicate rule, will
be a huge nigger and Chinese province with
a few white men to boss things, and keep
order, and collect revenue. Population must
be obtained to rent or buy the lands and
work the mines and make the great tropical
province a profitable speculation. The kind
that is wanted in such cases is one that is
servile, abundantly procurable, cheap, which
is accustomed to hard conditions and can
live in a sultry climate, or, if it doesn’t live
to any great extent, can die there peaceably
and without attracting much attention, and
be easily replaced. Also, it must be a popu-
lation which won’t write to the papers and
complain about the syndicate’s doings, or
clamor for a Parliament and a share in the
work of Government, or send ugly memorials
to the British authorities and distribute
copies thereof to the press, or otherwise
cause publicity and render itself objection-
able. In short, it must be nigger and
Chinese in race and instincts, for no other
kind that is easily obtainable would fit
in with the syndicate variety of government.
To fill the Territory with a white population
would utterly upset the arrangements ; such
a population would insist on all manner of
rights which would interfere with the profits
and deprive the syndicate of its control, and
presently of its ownership, of the country,
and if it was ruled according to the methods
of chartered companies the inevitable result
would be revolution. • Moreover, the re-
quired population—which must be procured
in a hurry in order to grind out the divi-
dends without delay—can’t be found in Aus-
tralia for a good many years to come,
and to import it from Europe at great
expense, when a cheaper and much more
suitable variety can be found close at hand
in Asia, would be sheer insanity from a
money-making point of view.
# • •
Then the syndicate’s cheap nigger-labor
and leprosy would begin to filter over the
borders into S.A. and Queensland and New
South Wales and Westralia. It could not
be kept out unless the adjacent provinces
were prepared to establish military pickets
over miles of the most desolate frontier
in Australia. Its arrival would be encour-
aged by all the squatters, sugar-planters,
farmers, mine-owners, and others who want
cheap labor—and their name is legion. It
would spread by degrees from the ever-
full reservoir of the Territory until
a cheap, servile, alien race had in-
vaded every labor - mart in Australia,
and heathenism, tribal wars, leprosy,
and Joss were everywhere, and a piebald
race began to grow up throughout the great
Piebald Land of Australia, The hope of
every good Australian is to keep this new
country for the white man, and avoid the
establishment here of the awful curse which
has descended on the Southern States of the
American Union, where the white man and
the black are at constant war, or the twin
curse which has fallen on some of the South
American republics, where the white man
and the black have intermingled until the
result is a mongrel race of no account, or
the still worse disaster of Hayti, where the
nigger and the half-breed and the Caucasian
are on top alternately, and the country is a
Bedlam. The syndicate, however, would be
troubled with no such considerations. Its
occupancy, in the natural order of things,
would only be temporary, for syndicate
government cannot last for ever under any
conditions. Its onl> object would be to
make money. It would in no way be con-
cerned with the social condition of its pro-
vince, for it would not live there. It would
care nothing what kind of a country it made
the Territory, or what kind of a country it
made Australia, for it would not be Austra-
lian and would have no shadow of interest
in Australia’s welfare. A Russian or French
or German or Japanese autocrat who came
to stay would have an interest in doing his
best for the country according to his lights.
The syndicate, which couldn’t stay per-
manently, would have absolutely none. It
would be the most soulless despotism on
earth—a company of purely foreign specu-
lators whose interest it would be to sell the
Territory piecemeal to all the scum of the
earth if it could make a profit thereby, and
then when the march of events made syndi-
cate government no longer possible—as it
always does sooner or later—leave the con-
sequences as an enduring curse upon Aus-
tralia.
• •
The Territory, when its English and
Hebrew and German and French and
mongrel owners got their scheme into full
blast, would be the Land of Cheap Labor
par excellence. It would consequently be a
land of cheap production, and it would com-
pete everywhere to advantage with the ex-
ports of white Australia. It would also, as
already mentioned, be the source from which
swarms of cheap nigger laborers would drift
into the white, or still comparatively white,
provinces. Its cheap nigger-made products
would cut out the white Australian in his
own markets, unless they were kept out by
a cast-iron Protective tariff. And if the
syndicate - governed province grew to
maturity it would probably object to this
treatment and demand concessions. In such
a case it might prove a formidable neighbor.
A State which doesn’t spend money on free
education and similar adjuncts of civilisation
—which is run on the beautiful Maxim-gun
principles of forcible occupation—and which
is bossed by an autocratic administrator in-
stead of by the divided councils of a Parlia-
ment, is always formidable if its forces
are well-directed. In the course of years,
with reasonable good fortune and with
great financial and political interests to sup-
port it at “ Home,” the syndicate which
ran the Northern Territory might become
almost as dangerous an enemy to provoke as
was the East India Company in former
times, or as the South African Company has
lately proved itself. Its existence would put
an end, for many years at least, to the pos-
sibility of an Australian Commonwealth, for
the syndicate would never join in a system
under which the Federal Parliament would
have power to prohibit nigger and Chinese
labor and to destroy its best sources of profit.
And it would put an end for ever to the
dream of a white Australia. Even if the
Territory did fall at last into the Federation
it would have to be taken over along with a
great alien population, which population
would inevitably claim the rights of citizen-
ship and demand a share in the political
control of this continent, and which couldn’t
by any possibility be sent back again to the
tea-fields and jungles of its fathers.
* * *
Great Britain has a paper claim to the
ownership of the Northern Territory. It
never did anything for that country ; it did
not settle it, nor open it up to commerce
and civilisation, nor spend any money upon
it, nor do anything whatever for its advance-
ment, and it made no claim upon it until
recent developments in the adjacent pro-
vinces opened up a prospect of its becoming,
at an early date, a much more valuable
possession than it has hitherto been. Yet it
has—on paper—an absolute right to do with
the Territory as it pleases : just the same
right as it has to establish an Inquisition
there, and convert the inhabitants to the
Anglican Church by boiling them in oil, if it
feels so disposed. And the Australian people
have a much greater right--one which doesn’t
exist on paper, and isn’t expressed on parch-
ment, but which is more enduring than any
other —to resist in one case as in the
other. All the empty forms of annexation
which were ever devised can give no
country a right to be an unspeakable
disaster to its neighbors for the benefit
of a gang of selfish speculators—and even if
they did it wouldn’t matter. If the Northern
Territory scheme takes definite shape, and
the Australian people do not resist it to the
uttermost, then they are not fit for national
existence and deserve anything that may
happen to them. There was never, in the
history of any British province, an aggression
which called more loudly for resistance to
the bitter end, and if the Australians endure
it they may safely be set down as a race
that is servile enough to endure anything.
Meanwhile, the danger is that South
Australia will let the matter pass
because the Territory is at present
only an unprofitable burden, and that the
Boodle Government in Queensland will view
it with complacency because the nigger-labor
and syndicate disease has eaten into its alleged
soul, and that New South Wales, Victoria and
Tasmania will be comparatively indifferent
because the danger is a long way off and the
Territory is to them practically an unknown
land. If they do they will only reap what
they have sown when the curse comes
home to them in after years. If the
scheme is a reality it is a danger a thou-
sand-fold greater than any other that
Australia has yet met with. If it is
merely a fiction intended to try the
temper of the community on the subject, it
is necessary to enter such a national protest
as will prevent any probability of the reality
coming along by-and-bye. In either case it
draws attention to the fact that the Ter-
ritory, under the present conditions, is a
standing danger, and until there is formed
a Federal Government strong enough to
insist on it being made an Australian pro-
vince, instead of a mere (anomalous fragment
of Britain, the real danger—even supposing
the present one should prove only imaginary
—may arrive at any moment.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Will You Walk Into My Treaty?
Therk is talk of a conference of Australasian
Cabinets to decide upon the action to be taken
in regard to the British-Japan ese-treaty signed
at London on 16th July, 1894. These provinces
are empowered by Article 19 of that treaty to
come under its provisions by giving notice to
the Japanese Government before Aug. 25, ’96.
And on 4tb Nov. a member of Brisbane Cham-
ber of Commerce moved :
That, in the opinion of this Chamber, the acceptance
by Australia of the treaty of 1894 would
develop commercial interests beneficial to the trade and
industries of each colony, promote friendship with a
treat Eastern power, and be a loyal and opportune
expression of the sentiment that British people, wherever
located, are part and parcel of one great empire.
Consideration of this motion was postponed,
but it was received with an approval which
other Chambers of Commerce would doubtless
share. As the ordinary commercial-man would
sell his own soul, if he had one, to gain a busi-
ness advantage, it seems sweet and honorable
to him to mortgage the souls of other people.
But it is worth while letting Australians under-
stand exactly on what terms Fat Man proposes
to coin their blood into drachmas. If any
Australian province agrees to come under the
provisions of the treaty it delivers itself,
bound hand and foot, to the Jap invader.
Says Article I.:—
The subjects of each of the two high contracting
parties shall ha full liberty to enter, travel, or reside in
any part of the dominions and possessions of the other
contracting party, and shall enjoy full and perfect pro-
tection for their persons and property.
The Jap will have precisely the same rights at
law as an Australian, “ ami enjoy all the rights
and privileges ” connected with the administra-
tion of justice “ enjoyed by native subjects.”
The Japs cannot be poll-taxed :
They shall not be compelled, under any pretext what-
ever, to pay any charges or taxes other or higher than
those that are, or may be, paid by native subjects.
The Japs are to have full freedom to deluge
this country with the products of factories
where from 3d. to 6d. a day is good pay for a
workman employed from 12 to 16 hours:
The subjects of each of the high-contracting parties may
trade in any part of the dominions and possessions of the
other by wholesale or retail in all kinds of produce, manu-
factures, or merchandise. . . . They shall have liberty
freely to come with their ships and cargoes to all places,
ports, and rivers . . . and shall enjoy, respectively,
the same treatment in matters of commerce and navigation
as native subjects.
Prohibitive duties must not be imposed on any
Japanese goods :
No other or higher duties shall be imposed on the im-
portation ... of any article . . . than on the
likeariicle produced or manufactured in any other foreign
countries.
In a word, the Jap, under the new treaty,
comes to Australia to stay and found Japanese
settlements. He must be given every right,
every privilege, which the native Australian
enjoys, and saddled with no penalty which is
not suffered by every white resident. He will
come in Jap-made ships, bringing an army of
Jap laborers to build Jap wharves and ware-
houses, and, discharge Jap goods. He will
build and work his own factories ; and the pro-
duct of those factories must be treated in
exactly the same way as the product of an Aus-
tralian factory ; and the white man who cannot
rear his family decently, after the fashion of
his country, on £1 a week, must submit to
absolutely open competition with the yellow
man whose family would be lapped in luxury
on =£l a month. If Australians consent to come
under the provisions of such a treaty, they are
bigger asses than The Bulletin takes them for.
Even a simple sheep does not hold up its
throat to the butcher and bleat to be killed.
But Australians are asked to deliberately pre-
sent to the knife the throat of their industries,
of their social organisation, of their children
and their race. They have all to lose and no-
thing to gain. What natural products—such
as wool and meat—Japan will take from us,
she will gladly take now, without any recipro-
cal treaty, simply because she needs them. The
only things we can takefrom her are the products
of a cheap labor with which it is hopeless for
white races to compete —products which will send
our children to the gutters, our women to the
streets, our men to the devil. And if a nation
with eyes in its head desires to see these things
come to pass at the bidding of the Trade-fetish,
and in order to fatten the paunches of huck-
sters, commission-agents, and middle-men, in
the Devil’s name let it.
A Legal Pooli Bah.
The present situation of Attorney-General
Want in the Dean conspiracy case is indeed
remarkable. The N.S. W. Attorney-General is
a species of three-headed animal—officially,
.that is—with occasional appearances as a four-
headed animal by w’ay of variety. It is he, in
the first place, who lays an information when
a prisoner is committed by the inferior Courts.
He is the Grand Jury, and decides whether or
not there is a case against the accused. Then
he prosecutes that individual, sometimes by
deputy, sometimes (as in the Dean case) per-
sonally, and demands his conviction with
impassioned rhetoric, and lays down his guilt
with a sickening thud before the jury. And,
when he is convicted, the same Attorney-
General, as an important member of the
Cabinet, is a very considerable element in the
Court of Appeal, and is called on to help in
deciding whether he was justified in laying the


Information to begin with, and whether he con-
ducted the prosecution fairly, and his evidence
was reliable, and whether he hadn’t been howl-
ing for the blood, or for the incarceration, of an
innocent man t Therefore, it naturally follows
that when the Attorney-General takes pro-
ceedings as a Grand Jury he must make a
great endeavor in his capacity as prose-
cutor to show that he did right in
his capacity as Grand Jury. And when
the case comes before him as a Court of
Appeal he must decide—if he is an ordinary
human being—that he did absolutely right in
his two capacities as Grand Jury and Crown
Prosecutor. And if the case comes before
Parliament on a final appeal he must argue and
vote, in his fourth capacity as a legislator, that
he did perfectly right in his other three
capacities, and bring all the weight of the
rascally party system to bear to make others
vote that way also. This is the Attorney-
General’s position at ordinary times, but in the
Dean conspiracy case Mr. Want has struck a
still more ridiculous and unfair attitude. For
this time only he, formally and in so many
words, abdicates his functions as a Grand Jury
in favor of the most wholly unfit man in all the
world —Mr. C. G. Heydon. (“If there is a
committal” —and there is—“ Mr. Want will
be guided entirely by what Mr. Heydon
advises.”) Mr. Heydon is unfit for the
one reason that he has acted as Crowii
Prosecutor in the case. In that capacity
he had to demand and plead and pray and
insist that Mr. Chick and the other accused
should he committed for trial, and to declare,
with a desperate earnestness of reiteration, that
there was a good case against them. Then, as
Grand Jury, he has to decide whether there is a
good case against them,andashe had to maintain,
as prosecutor, that there was such a case, there-
fore lie is the one man in all the world who
can’t possibly—unless he is more than human —-
be an unbiassed Grand Jury in this particu-
lar instance. By delegating his authority
to the only man whose position makes it almost
impossible for him to view the case dis-
passionately, Mr. Want makes the previous
unfair position even more unfair than it was
before. The moral of it all is that the Attorney-
General should be prosecutor only. His duties
as Grand Juror should be entrusted to other
hands, and he should be outside the Cabinet, so
that lie won’t sit as a court of appeal to judge
his own conduct. In the present position of
affairs every intelligent person in Australia, for
a variety of reasons, must deeply sympathise
with Mr. Crick.
The Unemployed.
Dear Bulletin, —The solemn fact, which
there is no good in glossing over, is that, of the
tens of thousands of impoverished men, young
and old, who have been hanging around Sydney
this last six or twelve mouths looking for jobs
or shifting sand at Centennial Park, not more
than a mere fraction are fit when called on for
a day’s hard competitive manual labor or a
day’s swift, skilful tradesman’s work. Master-
tailor Brown, say, wants a couple of men for a
hurried job, and informs Cheer’s bureau. Two
men are quickly in attendance and are given
needles. They were once decent craftsmen ;
but that was before they were driven to sleep out
o’ nights ; before they were drenched with rain
and pelted with wind ; before they pegged with
the pick and hoisted with the shovel, and tried
other precarious and unusual labor. Now their
hands are a-tremble, and Brown, after watch-
ing them for an hour, pays them off, and writes
a blistering letter to the inoffensive Greer,
who will show you lots of such scornful epistles.
Again, Ganger Smith wants half-a-dozen men
to complete a party to clear a site for a six-
storey warehouse, and Greer responds with
alacrity. But what can his six men do along-
side the regularly-employed and regularly-fed
pick-and-shovel men of the gang? They go at
the work with a will, but the indifferent diet
of the last six mouths soon gives them
away. The precarious shelter of windy and
rainy nights tells a tale, and presently
Ganger Smith yells, “Here, lads, go up and
get your money ; you won’t do I ” Gauger
.Smith is the voice of the merciless system
which demands a certain quantity of work per
man, and if lie doesn’t produce that, away goes
his employment. “ No tyranny is so grinding
as that of the poor man who is forced to oppress
the poor.” And as with the tailor and navvy,
so with the typesetter, the watchmaker, the
clerk, and most of the other unemployed.
These will never be absorbed by current pro-
cesses. Masters, to continue masters, must
reject such laborers. Current processes must,
therefore, be set aside or radically improved.
In the Dutch industrial settlements this feature
of the unemployed difficulty is carefully
studied. Weakened and emaciated men are
taken in and worked from grade to grade in the
settlements until in the end they are turned
out equal to the average of their craft. No
Government Bureau should offer to outside
employers workmen in the mental and bodily
condition of the great majority of the crowds
now seeking work. If the offer be accepted,
the master is a fool, or he intends to cut down
the regular rate of wages.—Yours, &c., X.Y.
The Real Gambling Evil.
Five or six years ago Melbourne had a sudden
ipasm of virtue, or snuffle, or something of the
sind, and the authorities proceeded to make a
dean sweep of the unlawful and dishonest
“totes.” It was a wonderfully clean sweep,
and for a whole week it was practically impos-
sible to find a working tote in all the boom-
stricken city. The better class of tote-keepers
—those who were moderately honest and so
didn’t realise enough out of the business to
make it worth their while to risk getting into
gaol—never came back, or at least very few of
them did, but the worse variety began to turn
up again a few weeks after that famous raid,
and have been there ever since. The
law never raised energy enough to make
another such clearance, and, moreover, the tote-
keepers, warned by that painful experience,
hired legal talent to pick large hole- m the
statute, and now by making their bets indoors,
and then taking the money on the pavement
outside—the pavement not being a “ fixed
place ” within the meaning of the Act—and by
other devices, they dodge the authorities effec-
tively. Melbourne now reeks and swarms with
fraudulent totes, and so does every other Aus-
tralian city, and the law is admittedly a poor,
impotent, busted thing which can do really
nothing to suppress the evil. It can sup-
press the honest consultations which had
a fixed address and gave everybody a fair
chance for his money, and the moderately
honest totes, and having done that it leaves
the hopelessly - fraudulent ones in posses-
sion of the field. The result is that they are
protected against their honest competitors.
This is not the result that was intended, but,
as it is the one actually arrived at, the Legisla-
ture might as well consider whether it is
not time to give up snuffle and deal with
things as they are. A country which raises
about two-fifths of its taxation out of grog
has no right to snuffle, anyhow. If the Aus-
tralian Governments licensed one or two men
of good character to run consultations in each
province, and made them deposit a handsome
sum with the Treasury as security, and
limited their profits to 10 per cent., one-third
or one-half of these profits to go to the State ;
and legalised the tote on similar lines ; they
would not make the gambling evil any worse
than it is, but the evil would be a lot cleaner
and more honest, and a good many hundreds of
greasy, bejewelled ruffians who now live by it
would be driven out of the business. Also, the
element of loafing and blasphemy and tobacco-
juice and stale whisky-breath which attends
the back-alley tote would be practically extin-
guished, and these things are sixty times more
demoralising than the gambling itself. The
tendency to lean surreptitiously on something
in an unkempt back-room, and curse and spit
and rub a greasy head against a dingy wall, is
the real curse ; the gambling itself is a wholly
casual and unimportant adjunct.
The Japanese Invasion.
Ed. Bulletin. -A few facts from one who lives
in a hot-bed of Asiatics may help to show Aus-
tralian workmen what they have to expect if the
new Japanese treaty is allowed to flood the
country with merry little brown men. I often
watch the Japs here at work, and frequently
employ them—simply because I must or go
without. I wanted a 6ft. Sin. x 2ft. Sin. x
lldn. deal door made—four panels moulded on
each side—O.G. 1J moulding. A Jap started
to make it by hand, had to rip and dress all the
deals by hand, and, with no one to help him,
had the door built in six hours, and well
finished off. He had to make the moulding.
The joints and mitre were well done ; the whole
was a very good door, and in my opinion made
in remarkably quick time. It was an imitation
of a Melb. machine-made door. The said Jap
is a quick and clever wood-turner, and I am
quite sure he could hold his own with a vast
number of Australians. I saw him turn 16 chair-
legs—by foot, and good pattern, I4in. turning
well done, clean and sharp edges, out of 2J x
'2\ cedar—in one hour. Put this to the average
wood-turner in Australia in a foot lathe, and
see where the Jap stands—an easy first. His
mate is a wheelwright, and he can build from
the rough two pair of four-foot wheels (known
in the trade as “ Ballarats ”) in six days of not
more than nine hours. He turns his nave,
segments his felloes, chops out his spokes (iron-
bark), mortices and bores by hand, and puts 14
spokes in—well-dressed, and pure imitation of
the English work. In this hot climate where’s
the Britisher who can sneer at this ? This man
is content to earn 20s. per week, and thinks
himself well paid. As laundry-men you can’t
beat the Japs, though they charge 4s. per dozen
pieces, large and small, and a pair of
socks counts two. As gardeners the Chinese are
an easy first, both in cultivation and product.
The Jap “ women ” (very numerous in Wes-
tralia) totally eclipse their white com-
petitors. They are particularly clean,
modest, sober, exceedingly polite, don’t
thieve, and look upon their calling in a
purely commercial sense. No white woman
can compete against those brown dots of
humanity. As shopkeepers the Japs are ex-
ceedingly polite and obliging—nothing is too
much trouble for them, and their stocks are
well selected and wonderfully cheap. As
tailors they are good, and even a Collins-st.
firm would not refuse to accept their work ; for
they can sew, cut truly, and perfectly. In
this line, however, they are not cheap, as the
material they sell is positively dear. They can
imitate anything—and go to work in a very
sensible manner —and their styles are an exact
copy of the English, or according to order. If
you ask a Jap his own opinion of his own goods
he will usually tell you approximately the
truth. In dealing I find them exceedingly
honest ; they can always pay up. Their living
—mostly dried vegetables and fish—costs a
trifle ; I positively assert that they are a more
formidable foe than the Chinese. They are
strong, game and ambitious, and co-operation
is their forte.— Cerhalos, Roebourne, W.A.
The Lost Selector.
Melbourne Age :
We cannot affiorU to forget that of the hundred thou-
sand odd selectors who have taken land in Victoria there
are only some 35,000 remaining on that land. The rest
have fallen victims to the mortgagee. The farming
business cannot pay 8, 10 or 12 per cent., and it has been
only a question of tune as to how long the land so encum-
bered takes to drift through the hands of the selector
into those of the mortgagee, and pass back into the pos-
session of the grazier as a freehold. There is no possi-
bility of denying the facts. Mr. Best has had some
very uncomfortable stories to tell Parliament on this
subject. In one instance a man held 32 selections,
having an area of 9275 acres, and bwing £3OO in rent.
This man was the mortgagee who got possession of the
land after it had been fenced, cleared and made ready
for cultivation. In another case a mortgagee got posses-
sion of 26 selections, covering 3539 acres, and owed £9Bl
in rent; another held 14 selections, comprising 3072
acres, and owed £913 in rent ; whilst another held 13
selections, embracing 2386 acres, and owed £735 in rent.
There was a long list, five mortgagees alone had driven
106 families off their holdings, had got possession of
23,634 aores, and owed the State £5323 in rent.
It is an acknowledged fact that the accumula-
tion of large estates in a few hands is an un-
mitigated evil. The following are four ways to
deal with it: (I) By the establishment of a
State Land Bank for the purpose of lending
money at low interest. The result will be that
the State will be the principal mortgagee, and
foreclosed properties will, to a great extent, fall
into its hands instead of into those of the private
Shylock ; (2) By a graduated land-tax, with
absolute exemption for the small owner, and a
steadily increasing impost for the large one until
the ownership of anything above, say, 10,000
acres of freehold will become a spec, that
will yield no profit whatever ; (3) By absolutely
prohibiting the ownership by any one indi-
vidual of more than a certain area. This law,
however, would probably be dodged so effec-
tually by the dummy system as to be of practi-
cally no account; (4) by an adaptation of
the French law of inheritance in a modified
form, it being enacted that any freehold estate
of more than, say, 2000 acres area or, say,
.£IO,OOO value, should be divided at the owner’s
death equally among his descendants. This
would be a painfully slow method of reform,
but it would get there in the course of a century
or two. But, for a community which wants to
tackle the evil now, the State Land Bank and
the graduated-tax—provided it is sufficiently
graduated—are the shortest and most efficient
ways out of the difficulty.
Government by Contradiction is beloved of the
Tories, and the spectacle is witnessed in Vic.
Assembly of a few stale and fulsome members
rising to complain that the Government is
giving them a tree hand in the discussion of
Government business. Is the mallee to be
occupied as freehold or leasehold ? The
Government bill provides for freehold, and the
anti-alienationists immediately join issue. A
Tory Government of the stubborn sort would
insist on the success of each clause being a
certificate of confidence in the Government,
and men would vote for freehold who believed
in leasehold, and men who didn’t care for either
would at the party-call be compelled to vote to
damn or save a Government. Voting by mob
is a feature of barbarism —the right of Demo-
cracy is not to merely say aye or no to some
Governmental accident—it is the right to
differentiate, to vote partly aye and partly no,
and partly neither. If mallee land is to be
selected for freehold, and Parliament agrees,
and then the Government proposes 640 acres
for selection, and the Opposition says 1000
acres, and an independent party says 320 acres
—it is still the right of any member to separate
himself from all these cabals, and vote for one
acre or acres. Government by Contra-
diction uags the individual, and hands over his
political soul to a political hell, where the
country’s interests are used for fuel; and the
Turner Government or the Reid Government
that bucks against this rotten system will get
honor and glory, even if personal flabbiness
and wobbling be the cause.
The Vic. Assembly has fooled away a consider-
able portion of the past five months in discuss-
ing a reduction of the existing duties on woollen
piece goods. The result of innumerable divi-
sions, “ rallies,” casting votes, and personal
explanations is a 10 per cent, reduction which
will operate from January 1, 1896. A further
fall of 5 per cent, is to happen two years later
if nothing occurs in the meantime to upset the
arrangement. Something will upset it, without
doubt. The time spent in maundering around
this bogus vision of the future has been utterly
and knowingly wasted. Next New Year’s Day
is somewhat close at hand, the present Assembly
may be expected to endure until then, and
the reduction of woollen duties to 30 per
cent, seems bound to come to pass. A
good few people in Victoria are looking
forward to the time when it will come to pass,
and expecting woollier coats and pants, or more
economical tailors’ bills from this particular
item of “Tariff Reform.” So many large
patriotic speeches have been spoken, so many
long leading articles written for and against
the 30 per cent, tariff, that it has come to be
regarded as a national blessing or a curse—one
or other of these things—as compared with a
duty pf 40 per cent. The Freetrade organ
calls it a step in the right direction, the Daily
Syme calls it a step in the wrong direction. To
the public it will make no difference at all.
The case of John Moloney, of Richmond,
Melb., as set forth in a petition presented to
Parliament, is a convincing sermon on the
Godliness of Thrift as applauded for years by
the resident Fat Man. John is over seventy,
has a crippled spine and a diseased hip, and is
a physical and financial ruin after giving Vic-
toria the labor of his hands for 50 years. In
the days of the boom Moloney owned £535
laboriously earned, and screwed together by a
due observance of the gospel according to the
paunchy Apostles of Thrift, but he listened
to the voice of the Boom Incarnate and in-
vested his all in paper banks and parchment
societies with enormous capitals in printers’
ink. In Moloney’s document “ your petitioner
prays ” that, inasmuch as he was beguiled to
throw his money into cracked banks and
bottomless financial institutions by seductive
and oleaginous M.’sP., Parliament will be
moved in its charity and wisdom to do some-
thing for him in his nfirmity and distress.
The public would have been delighted to have
been saddled with the keep of the boomers for
a few years, but they have no such hospitable
feelings for the boomers’ victims.
The New South Wales public is anxiously
waiting to hear some more about the Slattery-
Anderson dispute. Anderson says that Slat-
tery appointed as Inspectors of Agriculture—
apparently for political reasons—incompetent
persons, drunks, and utterly useless characters,
and appointed them at needlessly high salaries
and despite the strong remonstrances of the ex-
pert at the head of the department. The bur-
den of a Parliamentary document just issued is
that Slattery says he didn’t commit these acts,
and Anderson says again, with renewed
energy and much more ugly detail, that he did,
and Slattery says some more than he didn’t.
There is also some talk about a select committee
of the Assembly to inquire into the matter, but,
so far as the published correspondence goes, ex-
Minister Slattery doesn’t actually, and in so
many words, ask for this committee, neither
have any of his numerous friends in the Assem-
bly whooped up the Government in the matter.
Consequently, the very interesting question
about the appointment of O’Hooligan,
Maloney and 00. seems to be in danger of
dropping out of sight a good deah
New South Wales Speaker and Chairman of
Committees possess, singular to say, under
the present forms of the House, more drastio
and absolute power over members than is
possessed by either functionary in the Com-
mons. Take an unfortunately common inci-
dent as an illustration. When a member
offends and is obdurate in New South Wales
Assembly, the Speaker or Chairman simply has
him run into the street. In the Commons the
Speaker or Chairman “ names ” the offender.
Thereupon the Premier or, in his absence, the
leader of the House, moves that the offender be
suspended, and votes are actually counted. In
the last case reported, that of Dr. Tanner, the
division took place although Dr. Tanner could
not find a supporter to act with him as
teller. “ The ayes have it,” announced
the Speaker, and the doctor was marched
out. Why should a New South Wales
Speaker or Chairman possess greater power
than this? Then, again, in N.S. Wales, the
member so marched out is actually thrust into
the street. In the Commons, however, a first
suspension only excludes him from the legisla-
tive chamber. The galleries are open to him,
and so are the lobbies and smoking-rooms.
The practice is that a first suspension excludes
a member from the legislative chamber for one
week, a second in the same session for a fort-
night, while a third excludes him for a month
from Parliament and all its precincts. This
looks, and is, a mode of dealing with fractious
members far preferable to the N.S.W. style ;
and why, then, is the prize-fighter method of
pitching a man into the gutter continued ?
The suggested conference between the two
N.S.W. Houses on details of the Land and
Income-tax Bill will, if it takes place, serve to
prove the value of such conferences as a solution
of bicameral perplexities. What are called
details in this case amount almost to fixed prin-
ciples, and, in the Council as well as in the
Assembly, the best legislative abilities are
harnessed to opposing propositions, so that a
harmonious result here would set a precedent.
In Great Britain, the Upper and Lower Houses
have long since abandoned the idea of deter-
mining any difficulty by a conference. Con-
ferences are occasionally talked of there, but
those experienced in the methods and claims of
the two Houses in England contend that a con-
ference between them is impossible where the
subject-matter is of importance.
Dear Bulletin, —Maoriland certainly pays
attention to minor details in her Labor legisla-
tion. That is her finest virtue. Let Sydney
D.T.’s indignant Britisher remember that he is
himself a pettifogging detail. Australians
wrangle about big measures that never pass, or,
if they do pass, are quite inoperative ; Maori-
landers approve radical laws, fill in “ minor
details,” and make their legislation workable.
The only perceptible dissatisfaction with these
arrangements is evinced by the fat shopkeeper,
the hateful factory-sweater, and the haughty
British tourist. The M.L. Early-closing Act
is obnoxious to these people, which is
the best possible argument in its favor.
In M.L., the workwoman works 48 hours
a week in a clean, well-ventilated building,
sanitarily complete, where even a dining-
room must be set apart for her use. The work-
woman doesn’t grumble at this ; she rather
likes it. Contrast with one Sydney sweating
establishment where recently each female hand
had thre'e or four apprentices—absolute raw
material—and a painfully heavy standard of
work to make-up. Employees started at 8.30,
were allowed half-an-hour for dinner, and for
weeks slaved almost every night until 8,9, or
10 o’clock —male hands working even on Sun-
days—for the whole of which overtime ordinary
day-rates were paid ; whilst apprentices were
benevolently granted Id. per hour for extra
work. Workers in M.L. are not gluttonous
enough to pine for this state of things. The
Telegraph correspondent’s “dispensation”
assertion is ridiculously dishonest, and down to
■ the level of a pooah fellah who couldn’t pur-
chase cigahs ahfter 1 o’clock on Wednesday,
dem it all, in beastly, degraded Maoriland.—
Thorold W.
The chaplain of Melb. gaol described the late
Emma Williams as a poor, ignorant creature
who had never had a chance to know or do the
right. Married at 14 years of age, she was a
mother at 15, and her husband knocked her
about. After she was brought to the gaol,
said her ghostly comforter, Emma Williams
gave herself up wholly to religion, and was
very grateful for its consolations. She went to
her death with resignation and a belief in the
Divine Mercy. Christianity, in fact, must
stand or fall by the assumption that Emma
Williams will be happy evermore, whilst any
other woman of her class, who shrinks from
murdering her offspring and is doomed to die
in an ordinary manner, must be damned for the
same period. Mrs. Besant’s creed seems to get a
good deal nearer to what ought to be the
terewth. This unfortunate criminal, says the
Theosophical Society, is born again, the child
of exceptionally cold-hearted, selfish parents,
and, perhaps, with a silver spoon in her
straight-cut mouth this time. Each attempt at
self-improvement in any one life is rewarded
with an easier chance of scoring in her next
innings, and so she goes working out her own
salvation. Deeming is working out his salva-
tion somewhere or other. Last time he was an
amorous plumber with a hankering after titular
distinction. the present moment he is a
real little lordling, as likely as not, or the babe
of a bloated brewer who has set his mind on
achieving a British beerage.

Class Taxation.
Thu cheap and shoddy Tories who assert that
tho graduated land-tax and tho absentee tax
have “ driven capital out ’* of S.A. and Maori-
land, and that the same driving-out process
must eventuate in N.S. Wales, are hereby in-
vited to consider tho case of dear old Britain.
In that country it is estimated that the land-
tax, local rates, tithes for the support of the
Established Church, income-tax (which is levied
on rents in addition to the tax on land), and
the succession duties, which are very heavy,
make up an average annual tax of Bs. 7d. per
acre on agricultural land. Agricultural laud is
very low down in the United Kingdom nowa-
days,* the Freetrade doctrine of the “ cheap
loaf” having reduced agriculture almost to
zero, and it is a common thing for all these
forms of taxation to swallow up 18s. in the £
of the net annual rental of country lands. It is
also by no means an unheard-of thing in parti-
cularly depressed localities for it to swallow up
all the rental. This is in the matter of bind
only, and altogether apart from the local taxa-
tion on house property. Then the Imperial
Government levies “ house duty ” in addition
to the Imperial land-tax and the local land
taxation ; also, it charges many other quaint
imposts, such as taxes on coats-of-arms, on
carriages, on the nominal capital of limited
liability companies, on fire-arms, house-agents,
on agreements for letting furnished houses at a
rent of more than £25 a year, on male servants,
gamekeepers, grocers, railway passengers, and
on a multitude of other persons and things. The
amount raised annually by imperial ami local
taxation in Great Britain is over £ 120,000,000,
or about =£3 2s. per head of the entire com-
munity'. This is, roughly speaking, about ss.
per head more than the amount levied in South
Australia, which, despite its graduated land-
tax and its income-tax, and other imposts
objectionable to the Fat Man’s soul, is the most
lightly-taxed province in Australasia. On the
other hand, it is less than the taxation in New
South Wales, Victoria, Queensland, and Tas-
mania, from none of which is it alleged that
capital has been “ driven about £2 less than
in Maoriland, where the total taxation equals
£5 per head ; and only about half that of Wes-
tralia, where it rises to nearly £6 per head,
despite which circumstance capital is flowing
in freely. But the really interesting circum-
stance is that nowhere in Australia is the
burden of taxation on agricultural lands,
houses, and incomes so heavy as it is in Great
Britain, therefore, if this kind of taxation is to
“ drive capital out” of Australia it would be
highly interesting to know why it doesn’t
iso drive it out of the “mother country.”
Jllso, when the local Fat Man complains
jf “ class taxation ” as a principle, he is
invited to consider the situation at ’Ome, the
place so dear to his Tory soul. There the
income - tax is Bd, in the £, and the
local taxation falls mainly on land, and
in addition the central Government raises
.£2,500,000 per annum by land and house
tax, and the estate, legacy and succession
duties on the property of the deceased Fab
Man ascend on a graduated principle to a
possible maximum of 18 per cent, of the total
property, and his investments are taxed in
every imaginable way while he is alive by
duties on bonds, debentures, the capital of
companies, the receipts of railway companies
from passenger-traffic, leases, conveyances, &c.,
and he is taxed on his house and his carriage,
and his coat-of-arms and his flunkey, and his
game keeper, aud his silver-plate, and his gun,
and many other of his gauds and luxuries.
Even the Maoriland Labor-party never carried
the principle of “ class-taxation ” further than
it has gone in dear old England ; all it has
done is to distribute the burden a little more
scientifically.
FiDiTOR Bulletin, —
Melbourne University’s Government subsidy now stands
at £9OOO endowment and £3250 annual vole.
What does the University do for this £12,250
per annum ? It imports a large and expensive
stall. Then it plans a time-table that will pre-
vent all but the children of the rich from
attending the professional lectures. It de-
teriorates from the ideal home of learning bo be
a mere training-ground for several professions—
law, theology, medicine, school-teaching, and
so forth. Nor is even this miserable function
well performed. The lectures are delivered
when the professor is well, and hasn’t got the
hump, and doesn’t want a holiday, and hasn’t
got business in the city. At lecture-time four
anaemic students appear and wait. If the pro-
fessor arrives (and he very often doesn’t) they
listen weakly and take obscure notes of a 10-
year-old lecture. Tbeu they go to another
lecture-room and give another professor the
chance to arrive, which very often he doesn't.
When it is thought probable that a new
lecture may be expected, some of the firms
who make money out of University shot t-
comings pay a trifle to some student
and be furnishes notes. These are repro-
duced with a jelly-pad, and smudgy
copies are sold at £3 3s. a set to
uudergrads who have too much sense to
cool their heels and sap their brains in the
lecture-halls, waiting for professors who so
often stay away. One professor recently
repudiated his lectures for a week, the
wretched students mustering as per table every
day. This is a typical case. The Univer-
sity needs radical reform or radical retrench-
ment. If it is to be part of the system of
State education, its time-table must not con-
tinue to operate against those who have to go
to their daily employment. Education at pre-
sent is degraded to be a stepping-stone to
certain polite trades ; though education for its
own sake is the real and reasonable object of n
university. And the door should be open to
the clever youth of the whole community, ami
not reserved for the gilded youth of Fat Man.
—Fluffy.

All the Sydney shows remain as described
last week, and for the present tMs column might
as well be closed for repairs. Meanwhile, one or
two changes are in prospect. “ Oal Kentucky,”
at the Lyceum, has decided to retire with the
dignity of a somewhat unappreciated spectacle,
and it is to be replaced on Saturday by Bronson
Howard’s comedy, “ One of Our Girls.” Which
of our girls it is is.not jet known, but The Bul-
letin can name the girl that it hopes it will be in
one try. B. Howard’s comedy has never before
been produced in Australia, and as Sydney hasn’t
seen a new comedj r for some time it should do
well, if it is anything litre up to its title.
** * *
“Robbery Under Arms” is living up to
the reputation which this paper gave it on the
opening night—as a financial success and a fine,
picturesque spectacle, with much gum-tree in it
and a very great deal of horse. Dampier’s mellow
voice is as musical as ever, and Alfred Woods’
aboriginal has lost none of his demoniac activity.
Woods’ aboriginal is an exhausting character ; all
through three long hours he is either arriving in
an impromptu fashion over a high gate, or else
departing at a furious run to see if the troopers
are coming—which they generally are —or else
racing aoross the stage on all fours in search of
“tracks.” Miss Julia Merton makes a particu-
larly clever old woman, though the said old woman
has no visible connection with the drama ; and
Mr. Watson’s Irish policeman calls for commenda-
tion as being every tiling that a serious-minded
real policeman should be. Several other persons
in a very long cast, who weren’t alluded to last
week, are hereby invited to consider themselves
mentioned in a more or less friendly spirit.
** * *
Afc the Tivoli, Hajek, the heavy-weight
man, continues to lift articles of considerable
tonnage in Ms usual mildly abstracted fashion, and
John Coleman has endeared himself to the great
soul of the public with sundry local songs—one
about a girl at Anthony Hordern’s being an
especial success. The yearning for “local
allusions ” is one of those things which can’t be
accounted for on any sane principle ; but the fact
remains that if a singer works in some remark
about a local drapery establishment he will bo
cheered till the roof is nearly blown off, while the
drapery shop itseif, is viewed by the same crowd in
a purely business light. James Harvey has returned
to this establishment, and his “imitations of well-
known actors ” are so remarkably good that anyone
but the actors themselves can recognise them at a
glance. No mummer ever knows how he looks on
the stage, and if he could get round to the front
and see himself from thee he wouldn’t know
himself from Sara Bernhardt.
** * *
George Leitch will shortly pack his big
spectacle in his carpet-bag for removal to some
other city. His vast panoramadrama is now
doing its last week at Sydney Royal, and whoever
wants to see the “Land of the Moa ” in all its
glory is advised to hurry up. The scenery in this
production shows all the really big thin-.s in
Maoriland except the public debt aud the female
anti-liquor movement, and the drama which
occasionally gets in front of the scenery is quite
as good as some other things this paper has seen
and survived.
** * *

The fact that the song about that unimportant
skin-complaint is now in its second week is suf-

George Rignold’s festive 1 season at Mel-
bourne Royal is progressing to the st'ains of a
vigorous brass band, whilst the British Flag leads
it on to glory. “ Youth,’’ last seen at the Ope'a
House in the days of a good while ago, was
reviv ed on Saturday, with George as the substan-
tial boy who goes astray. It is true th t he has
outgrown the name-part in a physical sense, but
in the matter of looking noble under difficulties,
and defying the wicked major to do his worst, the
only G. R. is quite as before, or more so. Mean-
while, Miss Kate Bishop has advanced to the
position of the hero’s mother, thereby strengthen-
ing one’s suspicion that the RRnold of to-day
must be tho sou of Ms fo mer self, who invariably
wooed and won the lady in question. Miss Bishop
is a specially comely and gentle stage-mother ; by
the way, also, Miss Maud Williamson is comely
without being gentle in the alluring guise of
an adventuress. Clifton Aldorson, the broguey
J. Sweeney and several other performers of vary-
ing capacity hold up the dialogue of this robust
production, when the story is not rendered in-
audible by cannons’ roars and shouts of British
triumph, and riotous music. At these times
dialogue would be very much de trap. “ Youth,”
as everybody kno*s, is mainly supported by im-
posing changes of scenery and large disciplined
bodies of auxiliary troops. And if everybody
doesn’t know it they had better hurry up and dis-
cover the historic fact for themselves, because the
days of the Rignoldian season are numbered, and
“ Youth,” alas ! is a fleeting dream.

The Oriental Carnival, after standing aside
for one day, to make room for another carnival.
has resumed operations on a rather vaster seals
of splendor than ever. The street in Jerusalem,
where the Hebrew maidens with largo lustrous
eyes and metal trimmings sell goods regardless of
expense, is about the liveliest thoroughfare on
record, especially in the evening when visitors

Juliet Flegeltaub Wray to a Smellbourne interviewer:
“ They say lam too thin in Sydney. Sydneyites like
fat people.” Not exactly, but they like the line of beauty
a little less defined.
An attempt to persuade themselves that they are only
gammoning to be mesmerised is evident in nearly all of
Kennedy’s paid subjects. Knowing what they will he
expected to do when under the influence, they try to
feel quite happy when the fat man tells them to stand
forward. After they have been made into lamentable
objects and brought round again they assume an air of
mental composure which must tickle Kennedy's fancy
immensely.
Mesmerist Kennedy got a well-deserved rebuke on
Derby night. A newly-discovered Melh. “ subject ’’ was
recalled to Ids proper self minus shoes and socks, and
with his feet and a portion of Ins pants in a bucket of
water. He demonstrated his disgust by emptying the
contents of the bucket upon Kennedy’s clean-boiled rag.
Toe bucket itself also followed the flying hypnotist, hut
it missed Ks mark—unfortunately.

F.: Bradfleid’s farewell verse—
“ We are leaving Victoria’s shore,
The kindest we’ve met,
We shall never forget,
For evermore,”
is “Poverty Pointed " as a painfnlly-weak gag, and so it
is—on paper. All gags are weak on paper. Mark Twain’s
first speech at Melh. journalists’ smoke-night was printed
in full in next day’s Age, and ttie general verdict was
“washy.” The joke about sheep in that speech was
reckoned more “ washy ’’ than the rest ; hut the dozens
of professional critics present at its delivery would have
cenified at the moment that it w-as ahso.utelv the best
joke ever conceived in this or any world. “When Dow
left his sheep-run it was a blessing to humanity—(.loud
laughter)—and to the sheep. (Sustained laughter).”
The cleverest landscape painting is rubbish if the atmo-
sphere he left out. The atmosphere of Twain’s gag was
his voice, his celelnity, Ids genius, Ids hair, and 1000
other things. Similarly, Bradfleid’s farewell, ungramma-
tical gag is nothing without its atmosphere—which con-
sisted of a catchy tune, a crowded house bursting with
heat, enthusiasm, and desire for flattery, a considerable
number of legs, &c. It was neither rondeau, villauelle,
nor triolet—it was a gag.

A PUZZLE.
Readers of The Bulletin in the country are often
puzzled to know where to send their orders, so
many drapery firms are advertising what they can
do, but few care much how orders are executed.
There seems to be a general idea that “anything
will do for the bush,” all forgetful of the fact that
ladies, even in the remotest backblocks, like to
have the latest styles and the freshest of goods.
Messrs. Riley Brothers, of 570 George-st., Sydney,
have for the past 30 years given special attention
to their Country Order Department, and know
intimately the requirements of every part of the
colony—North, South, or West. If the lady
readers of The Bulletin will look at this corner
of the paper every week they will see what we are
sending away every day. Gentlemen will find
articles for them in the corner of another page.
The largest stock of Nuns’ Veilings in the city,
in every shade of color, all double width, beautiful
fresh new goods for blouses or evening wear, 10id.,
per yard.
Muslins for blouses and dresses. Shower of
Hail pattern, ; Tambour Spots and Stripes
in White, Cream, Heliotrope, Pinks, Blues, all at
6Jd. per yard.
Large size Print Blouses, Is. 6d., Is. lid. ; and
so ne choice Muslin at 2s. 6d. each.
Riley Bi ■os., SGS and 570 George-st., Sydney.

The new circus-lent for Fitzgerald Bros.’ Maoriland
tour is the biggest ever made in Australia, and cost £3OO.
B. & B. have boon doing splendidly in M.L. At Auckland
they played “Mrs. Tanqneray,” “The Importance of
Being Earnest,” “ Rebellious Susan,” “Ideal Husband,”
“Niobe,” and “The Amazons.”
Fitzgeralds’ show, after a season in Brisbane, leaves for
Auckland. An enormous tent lias been built so that the
fair Roumanian may have room for her 130-feet dive from
the trapeze, and the professor may do his vasty drop into
the tank. These two items require a very high roof, and
the new tent towers upwards like a steeple. The circus
and menagerie rejoin the co. in Maoriland and return
with it to Sydney, so that the Fitz. brothers will run a
triple show on their next Australian appearance.
