Melbourne,
November 12, 1895.
My Dear Moorabinda—
Melbourne Cup is one of the few functions
which may be talked about a week after it s over,
the reason being that Cup styles are not for a day
only, but show how women are going to look ail
the summer. Judging by tlie Cup, the hue ol the
magpie will have it all its own piebald way for it
was as I pronliesied it would be months ago when
I saw Grace’ Palotta’s black-and-white dress in
“Joe”—a black-and-white Cup. The styles
were “ artistic ’’—immense, floppy sleeves, full
skirts, long gloves, and unspeakable headgear, in
which every flower blossomed but violets, which
are tabu, though roses were the principal wear.
Taking it by and lai'ge, it was a tastefully-dressed
Cup, and one of the most successful. Tlie crowd
was enormous, and partly hid the tact that Flem-
ington lawn was not so green as last year, owing
to the drought, though the flowers were in great
fettle and all the arrangements quite perfect. Mr.
Byron Moore has added perfume to the well-
appointed retiring-room for ladies, and the supply
of pearl-powder therein shows that the V.R.C.
secretary knows a tiling or two. And it isn t
the Cup girl’s fault if every man Jack isn t also in
the secret as to where our peachy bloom comes
from. To see women assiduously powdering their
noses while men look on enraptured trom the door-
way is quite one of tlie Cup sights.
The vice-regal party presented a butterfly
appearance. Lady massey wore ivory-white silk
mingled with porcelain-blue and embroidered
white chiffon, pink and white bonnet; Mrs. Free-
man Thomas (who is the beauty of the V.R.
group) looked ethereal in pale-pink and green
covered with muslin and an airy toque; Lady
Maud Warrender wore mauve with a covering
(everything is covered nowadays, like unused
drawing-room furniture) of creamy, lacey stud;
hat in black, pink and green. Miss Kitty Dailey
wore blue and white stripes and a white hat, and
another V.R. vjsi tress was unspeakably airy in
white and green. Present fashions don’t look
warranted to stay. A good shower on Cup-day
would have reduced most of the gauzey belles to
mere parti-colored pulp. Tlie appearance ot some
of the vice-regal men was amusing : one wore
corsets, which gave him quite an hour-glass out-
line ; another, in a blue shirt, white collar, check
tie, yellow vest, and red bandanna handkerchief
hanging half out of his breast pocket, suggested a
music-hall artist who sings a razzle-dazzle lay. I
am not disposed to say kind things about the Cup
men from a sartorial standpoint. One youth wore
a blue shirt with no starch in the hnzzum and a
bunch of valley lilies to call attention to it; and
most of the headgear suggested “ where did he
get that hat ? ” Fortunately, men were in the
minority —fittingly enough, Auraria’s Cup was
crowded with women.
The absence of the fat woman in the favorite s
colors made doing the lawn a restful optical
experience, and people who sinned sartorially
erred more on the side of snuffiness than over-
gorgeousness. A Sydney society spinster with a
marsupial nick-name was an awful example m a
gown the color of a barrel of ashes, but her sister
in a black skirt and blouse shot in blue, pink, and
green, and Gaiety toque of many colors, was up-
to-date. Miss Marcia Cox, in pearl grey silk,
with a hairline of. black-pink velvet cuffs and
collar with rows of Valenciennes lace, black beef-
eater bat, trimmed with pink ribbons and black
wings, looked very smart, but was swamped by
her chaperone—Lady Clarke —whose confection
of oosmea-piuk ribbon brocade, with white chiffon
bodice elaborately adorned with silvery passe-
menterie, and gay little bonnet modishly unmatch-
ing, was quite tlie most stylish turn-out on the
lawn. Mrs. Rupert Carington encored the pink
gown she wore during the A. J.O. Spring Meeting.
Melbourne’s particular beauty wore a black skirt
with a floral-silk blouse.
The most refreshing group on the lawn was four
maidens gowned in the frilly, fluffy, skirt-
dancing style in which an enterprising girl might
point her toe at the grand-stand without going
against the grain of her toilet. Two of these
damsels wore salad-green, and two white, and it
was as good as a sea-breeze to meet them, they
looked so fresh. One or two tailor-made damsels
looked as if their Cup dresses hadn’t got home in
time; and a widow, in all the show of deep mourn-
ing and a widdy’s bonnet—a widdy just begin-
ning to “take notice,” judging by the white
ruffles at her wrist—did the lawn assiduously,
although, judging by the freshness of her crape, ,
the late-lamented must have been a very recent
corpse. How considerate of him to go and die
just in time for bis disconsolate relict to sport her
becoming weepers (ber “To Let” advt., as it were)
at tbe Cup ! The fat woman turned up in white
muslin with a blue spot, and rainbow ribbons
down her balloon sleeves; and another welter-
weight sported bright Reckitt’s blue, with a bold
white pattern, as if she were in training to be a
dinner-service. Tlie most glaring costume was
worn by a Sydney society-matron, who mingled
Coolgardie yellow, prickly-pear, magenta, and
pantomime trimmings of silver in a highly fashion-
able but inartistic way.
There was a good show of he-Sydneyites, like-
wise Brisbaneites at the Cup, but I don’t
intend to catalogue them, for Heaven knows
bow many happy homes might thereby be
wrecked. Many a devoted husband, who was
supposed to he in quite the ,opposite direc-
tion, was doubtless on Flemington lawn. I saw
a dozen grass-widowers in as many minutes—
one had a perfect screamer in tow, with golden
hair (that the merest jerk would have sent hang-
ing down her back) and a most expensive com-
plexion. I wouldn’t name for the world a grey-
headed youth, who saw the Cup leaning upon a
presentation umbrella, with an inscription on the
handle, “Presented to Dash Blank, Esq., by the
members of the Christian Endeavour Society,” or
words to that effect. By the way, the Sydney
youth who married a Gee Gee was visible at tlie
Cup—bis Gride wearing a black gOwn with a pom-
padour pink fichu, and her hair apparently hang-
ing on one hairpin under her romantic hat. But
the most noticeable men on Cup-day were two
higb-cliuroli parsons, who ostentatiously had cham-
pagne for lunch.
I think the V.R.C. authorities ought to insist
upon their lady visitors going through some sort
of umbrella - drill before they allow them to
promenade on the lawn or the crowded asphalt—
for fevv women are to be trusted with parasols in
a Cup crowd without endangering a great deal of
millinery, not to speak of false fronts. I saw one
poor woman hooked on either side like a shark,
and everybody’s hat was screw-wrenched during
the day by women with unmanageable gamps.
One victim had her toupe pulled slantwise and
had to beat a retreat, and the reckless way the
parasol brigade carried on was a constant menace
to millinery. And, apropos of umbrellas, scarlet
would seem to be out of fashion, the modish
bright colors being magenta —which casts a flat-
tering light on a pale complexion, but should be
avoided by the woman with a boiled prickly-pear
cheek —and butter-yellow, from which the bilious
would do we'l to refrain.
A very “ best seconds ” sort of crowd turned up
to tbe first Brassey garden-party at Melb. Guv-
mentouse. Like gleanings on an olive-tree w§re
the fashionable folk, for tlie gowns to be admired
had nearly all debuted at the Cup. Lady Brassey
wore her Cup array, which was a refreshing cir-
cumstance to be remembered by many a house-
father, giving the lie economic as it di*d to the
rumor that Lady Brass* y never wore the same
gown twice. Paint and powder are religiously
eschewed by Melb. vice-regal ladies ; and another
pleasant circumstance is that there is an embargo
on kissing vice-regal infants, for nobody was
allowed to slobber over the small Helen, though
several matrons had to hold themselves back with
both hands. It is well that Guvmentouse puts its
foot on baby-hugging—it will make it unfashion-
able, and so relieve many a child-hater from having
to pick out a clean spot on the flabby cheek of
Budge or Toddie.
The pictorial effect of the Garden Party was
rather spoilt by the plethora of strange youths in
office suits and straw hats. The women, of course,
were gaily caparisoned. Melbourne’s belle looked
very well in a very uncommon and dainty dress of
white and black ; Lady Brassey’s double sported
green witli a white-dot chiffon bodice with
jet ornaments; but the world is aweary of smoothly
bevelled rags, and there was more amusement in
the overdone brigade, beginning with a bright
yellow girl with a trickling sort of figure who sug-
gested tlie first stage of Welsh rarebit, and on I m i nat-
ing in a short fat woman, the shape of a Chinese
ohest-of-drawers, who was attired in pea-green
with purple gloves. Stained glass looked aiiSßinio
beside her.
The appearance of Lady Brassey wheeling
briskly along on her bike, attired in a neat brown
skirt costume and small brown hat, has no doubt
emboldened the leading Melbourne bicycle-club
to forbid lady members of the club from attending
the club runs in knickerbockers, as “other ladies
object to meeting such women.” So, thank
Heaven, it lias been definitely decided that cycling
damsels may only wear breeches en famille round
the front garden, and that a member found bifur-
cating in a public thoroughfare will be struck
off the rolls. Which excellent resolution will
not cleanse the Melbourne streets of all the
gridiron girls in knickerbockers (frequently of
home-made cut and lumpy to look upon), for all
the wheel women don’t belong to the Cyclists’
Union ; but, henceforth, when you meet a bifur-
cated damsel in Melbourne, you will know just
where she comes in bicycling social circles.
And apropos of New Women—it will probably
turn some of’em green with envy to learn that
baritone Brownlow is accredited with having 21
pairs of trousers.
The Gee-Gees tilled openly at parsondom. In
the first play it was frankly described as “dull,
but respectable in the second, tlie buffoon of the
piece was a parson ; in (he third, the whole of the
second part was almost given over to showing what
a waste of money the charity bazaar leads-to, and
how some of the money never gets as far as it is
expected to. But charity really dues begin at
home in very many instances —begins and ends
there, in fact. lam thinking now of a suburban
five-siiilling hop recently got up to aid the starv-
ing poor, or some such deserving folk. It had
been an unparalleled success in cheap manage-
ment —almost everything had been a free gift—
but when the committee’s little “working exes”
were handed in they came to just over £25.
After all, women are not so easily frightened,
or else the recent cable-train accident, which re-
sulted in a young lady, who was riding on the
dummy, being crushed to death by a cart, would
have cleared all the girls off into a safer carriage
for a time, at least. But you see them tilting
along as usual, insensible to the danger of dummy-
riding in the narrow and congested Sydney streets.
It is really dangerous for women to ride outside
on a cable-car (it’s not so bad for more athletic and
rationally-dressed man, who can jump off or over
more easily), and the chance you run of having a
horse’s heels in your face or a cab sitting in your
lapis too obvious to make a dummy-ride down
King-st. a special treat. The carter, who was the
alleged cause of the death referred to above, has
been committed for manslaughter on a charge of
“negligent driving,” but the driver wouldn’t
always be to blame.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent :
“ Affairs on board the Sunbeam were conducted
on ‘ Bab Ballad ’ lines. : Baron Brassey models
himself on the famous Capt. Reece, of the Mantel-
piece, whose kindness to his crew has become
classic. As soon as the Sunbeam readied port
the Governor fitted out six of bis sailors for a
fortnight’s cycling expedition into the country :
he hired the bikes and supplied coin for expenses.
On his first appearance in a Melb. theatre, the
new Gov., seeing his crew in the amphitheatre,
looked up and nodded genially to them.
“Now that the Gaiety girls are gone, one can
tell how a moonstruck Melb, youth wasted his
substance in champagne suppers, ostrich-feather
fans, &c., to gain favor in the sight of the alleged
maidens, and how the maidens enjoyed the gifts,
but laughed at the lovelorn loon ; also, of how be
sent his photo., in a silver-beaten frame, to one
damsel, who cut the picture out and returned it to
the donor, but kept the frame! Woman always
keeps the frame.
“ According to a darksome rumor a winsome
theatrical visitor has found that the charms of a
wealthy Australian appeal to tier more strongly
than the constant devotion of her English johnnie,
to whom she lias been betrothed for some time.
So johnnie has been * sacked,’ and Melb. girfs now
bewail the capture of one of the few rich local
partis.
“ For fear they should tumble over each other’s
swords and come to blows, the Brassey aidekongs
have allotted themselves specific functions.
Shaftesbury is to be the official attendant on
H.E., and will swagger by ids side when he opens
and closes Parliament, &c.; Freeman Thomas is
to be pa-in-law’s social master of the ceremonies ;
and the limp Lord Richard Neville is to generally
hang round and be ornamental. Wallington is to
be taught his place as private secretary—and no
frills.
“The enthusiasm of the Melb. crowd, who
chased Lady Brassey when she went shopping,
evoked scant sympathy from the vice-regal
lackeys. One diminutive buttons, in particular,
standing on his perch, with his small nose turned
up to its very utmost in disdain at the ‘ owdacious
behaviour of those horrid kerlonials,’ presented the
most overwhelming example of offended dignity
ever seen in Bourke-street.
“Gov. Brassey, if they ask him prettily, will
lend a selection of his pictures to the Melb. Art
Gallery for a season. But not just yet.
“ People noticed at Gov. Brassey’s first ball)
that H.E. greeted his guests with ‘ a sailor’s
grip ’ in place of the artificial and almost insulting
elevated-pump-handle shake of the Hopetoun
regime.
“ That wily showman, Capt. Wallington, mani-
pulates his vice-regal puppets with ever-increasing
skill. Thanks to his tuition, the Brasseys faced
their first Cup crowd strong in the consciousness
of sartorial gilt and spangles, and with a reper-
toire so rich in nods, becks, and wreathed smiles
as to suggest thorough rehearsal. Successful as
was his pupils’ Flemington debut, however, the
canny entrepreneur saw the races through a mist
of ambitions musings. Fired by the effect of that
threadbare-popularity device, the vice-regal march
from carriage to Governor’s box amidst slow
music and hat-drill, he yearned to administer a
double dose of the loyalty stimulant. The
result was seen at the vice-regal ball, when
the opening ceremony took the form of a
triumphal procession through the ranks of the
awe-struck guests, “up the middle and down
again.” The array, which included the whole
house-party, small-fry no less than big-wigs,
marched in Noah’s Ark formation, though,
unlike the pioneer geologist, Sec. Walling-
ton had decreed the separation of the sexes,
except in the case of Gov. and Lady Brassey,
who walked together. On the strength of the
alleged ‘pleased with a rattle, tickled with a
straw’ propensities of Melb. society, orders had
been issued for full war-paint. Nevertheless, the
1200 loyal invitation-holders took the patriotic
pageant pretty calmly, though there were specta-
tors who gazed lowly and reverently even upon
the dapper aides, mincing along arm-in-arm.
“ At the Cup, the inmates of the vice-regal box,
women as well as men, rose automatically to
their feet whenever Lady Brassey entered the
portals; but apart from this air of discipline
the Gov. House party, especially the ladies,
looked happier than in the days when the
sporting Countess, oblivions of ber kind,
sat watching the races till her eyes stuck to her
field-glasses. Mrs. Freeman Thomas, a gny little
brunette witli the “gift of the gab” like her
mother, the first Lady Brassey, was responsible
for a good deal of stir, and, at the moment of
departure, ran down the line of carriages to take
her place in the last on the list, with no more
dignity than was convenient. Low, broad head-
gear requires the hair to be “spread ” at the back
to correspond, instead of tapering to a point or
“tea-pot handle,” according to present Melb.
methods, so both the Brassey women affected a
promiscuous siyle of coiffure, little.—“sausage-
rolls’’ and “snail shells” scattered inconseqnently
here, there and everywhere, all over the back of
tile bead.
“So far as the new suite is concerned, Lord
Richard Neville is unanimously pronounced the
pick of the basket. Unlike young Shaftesbury,
whom Melb. maidens promptly classified as “a
big good-natured boy,” unduly addicted to an old
grey coat and shepherd’s plaid tie, Ids colleague is
recognised as a genuine johnnie with all the latest
improvements, such as white gloves at the races.
SAPPHO SMITH.
One prominent Yic. Justice frequently
pares his nails while listening to arguments of
counsel. “He apparently thinks he has the law
at his fingers’ ends,” said Purves the other day.
Tbe portrait of ex-Premier Parkes and bis
new bride which appears in another part of this
issue is reproduced from a photo, by Freeman and
Co., of 318 George-street, Sydney. The original
is a fine and most characteristic portrait of the
ancient politician. Freeman and Co.’s work is
the perfection of high-art photography, and in
delicacy of treatment, accuracy and finish this
firm has absolutely no superior.
The Melbourne official doctors would not
certify, in so many words, that Mrs. Williams,
hanged last week, was not pregnant; they would
only say that in their opinion such was not the
case. All doubts in the matter are said by Hr.
Shields to have been removed by tbe post-mortem,
which clearly proved that the woman was not
pregnant. How ever, the guarded way in which
the ante-mortem medical opinions were expressed
support the opinion held by many people, that
where an absolute and unqualified assurance can-
not be given in such a matter the life of the woman
should be spared.
Under all tbe circumstances, it is most
inexpedient that the Dean alleged-conspiracy case
should be tried before any N.S.W. Judge.
Reasons which easily suggest themselves render
it highly desirable that nobody mixed up, how-
ever indirectly, with any of the eases which have
recently caused so much debate should hold the
balance between the Crown (i.e., the official
lawyers) and the accused. There is a strong
Queensland precedent for borrowing a Judge to
adjudicate in the matter.
In lhe foremost rank of colonial Premiers is Mr. Reid,
of N.S.W. To look a greater fool than he is seems to he
one of his dearest wishes, and he enjoys the reputation of
being the lean ideal of a “Piccadilly Johnnie.” He ia
fond of theatre-going, and Ids eyeglass an<; , eneraliy fop-
pish exterior enable the audience to dislir. uish him at
once.
“Another instance of Mr. G. H. Reid’s cnu ing for
the unattainable,” at once snarls ( lie cynic. Here
is some more about G.H.R. up to date. He is
laying on flesh by the pound, and must have
gained 201 b. since taking to the Premiership,
Which growth is attributed by some people to his
infantile love of sweets. It is said that up to a
few years ago he would walk a mile to buy toffee.
The death of a nonagenarian spinster*
Miss Moore, at Sale (Gippsland, Vic.), last week-
was announced along with the deeply-buried in,
telligence that she was own sister to a past N.S,
Wales Attorney-General. Probably, not a soul
outside of the relatives of the recently-deceased
spinster remembers Attorney-General Moore (he
flourished as far back as 182(1), who left no foot-
prints on the sands of Cornstalkian time. Mr.
Moore is more completely forgotten his
sister, who, half a-centnry ago, kept a girls’ school
in Lyons-terrace, Sydney, and most of whose
pupils have since married the worms.

The N.S.W. Minister of alleged Justice,
when asked whether it was true that between
Rofe’s conviction and sentence his counsel had a
private interview with the Judge, and, if so,
whether it was customary forjudges, while con-
sidering sentence, to grant interviews to the
defending counsel of convicted persons, re-
plied that he declined to make any inquiries
into an innuendo made against a Judge.
If, he added, any member had a charge against a
Judge, he should submit it in the proper way (i.e.,
by specific motion). Considering how the Ministry
of which Mr. Gould is a member has blocked the
discussion of member Price’s specific motion against
Judge Windeyer, attorney Gould certainly takes
the belt for impudence. Another thing : it would
have been easy and proper for Minister Gould to
say (if he could say it) : “Prisoner’s counsel did
not privately interview the Judge after conviction
and before sentence.” The adjournment of. the
House should certainly be moved in this matter,
the arrogance of official lawyerdom having reached
an unbearable pitch.

To stand in our Sovereign Lady’s place.

UNRIVALLED.
Society
What is that long, low murmur that we
hear in Botany Bay? It is the members of the
official-legal ring whispering to one another.
And what are those piercing yells ? Those
are the yells of the Goddess of Justice, whom the
legal ring is assaulting, “ Thud ! ” that is the
Ring kicking her ribs in with its boots. “Wal-
op! ” Don’t you hear the poor woman’s skull
cracking on the pavement!
# * *
Even a rudimentary sense of justice sug-
gests that the Public Treasury should, in some
way, acknowledge responsibilities not only to
Mrs. Butler, but to other less sensationally-
wronged victims of blundering or blunderingly-
administered laws. The dogma that “ the State
can do no wrong ” is as dead as the dodo for the
present generation; and when the State is found
guilty of having assaulted innocent men or women
and damaged them by casting them into gaol or
scandalising their characters, it should be com-
pelled to pay over a sum of money in compen-
sation, just as any other aggressor is. In addition
to making for justice, this acknowledgment of
responsibility by the State might tend to curb the
precipitancy of Judges and stimulate the intellect
of jurors.

The Queensland Boodle Government had
an idea the other day. There has been much
trouble with the mild and reliable Kanaka around
Bundaberg; a few whites have been killed, and
mangled after death for pure cussednesa, and
women have been insulted and outraged, and
there have tribal fights with cane-knives and
hoes and clubs and the like, and other annoy-
ances. So the authorities invited the local
planters to send all their hard cases into Bunda-
Photo, by Freeman.
Ex-Premier PAPKES and his new wife.
oerg on a certain day, and in response a squad of
beetle-browed niggers rolled up with murder in
theireyes. The Polynesian Inspector or someother
peal Bumble harangued this scowling mass of
B avagedom in pidgin English, and urged its com-
ponent parts to be better niggers in future and
“ no killee some more white man,” or words to
that effect. If they did the big white fellow
Government would be very cross indeed. Then
each possible black homicide received a drink of
ginger-pop and a BUN. Imagine, if you can, the
senile decay of the Government which tries to
tame a howling, bloodshot Polynesian with a
bun ! As well endeavor to soothe the Inquisition
with an acid-drop.
The same week sundry of the bun and ginger-
pop tribe were tried for the murder of a white
man. The following is a sample of the evidence
(given by “ a Sunday-school teacher ”): —
We been stop longa road close up longa house belongs
Trevor; me see ’em white man go longa spring cart
longa road, leave ’em house longa Trevor behind him ; he
go stay house longa Mr. Noakes’ way ; me see ’em man ;
he walk longa road, close up Mr. Trevor’s place ; Jack
Harrung he get up and strike him longa tomahawk two
time longa front; man he fall down ; Peter he strike him
two time longa neck longa tomahawk ; Bennie he strike
him longa back two time longa tomahawk ; Curra Curra
he strike him longa tomahawk two time longa neck ; me
strike him longa nulla nulla longa head two time only;
after we kill finish him we altogether see him white man ;
we fellow altogether come home longa house longa half-
past 10 ; we fellow go to sleep ; we fellow burn ’em toma-
hawk and nulla nulla together ; some fellow plant ’em
head longa tomahawk ; me no savee w here he put him.
Think of one bun apiece in a case like that !
* # *
This year’s Melbourne Cup was a com-
paratively dry and dusty function. Writer, who
“did ” the lawn, and hill, and flat, saw only 11
drunks —six of them females. The most sensa-
tional spectacle of the day was provided by three
girls, all apparently under 16, sitting in a seclude
part of the flat, drinking bottled beer from the
original bottles, and all exuberantly “squiffy.”
On the lawn, clothes-horse was a greater favorite
than ever, and was easily first all day. In fact, so
far as the lawn is concerned, the Cup is rapidly
becoming an event of no importance whatever,
and the first Tuesday in Nov. will presently be
remarkable, from the point of view of the lawn-
frequenters, only as the day of the great annual
dress-parade, when half the women of Melbourne
advertise all they owe to their dressmakers.
To the Melbourne shop-girl Cup Day is now
what the Church anniversary day is to her little
sister in the country—an oecason for a big sartorial
burst. She saves up through the previous 12
months for a few hours of high life on the lawn,
and there she was to be seen by the score on the
sth, parading, two by two, along the grass-plot,
flaunting her pompadour silks, her zephyrs, mus-
lins, and chiffon in pert self-satisfaction, com-
peting successfully with the female representa-
tives of the “first families.” Under the elms, too,
the small shopkeeper run np against the customer
who couldn’t, to save his life, square that little
account for bread or meat or groceries, and yet,
ornate with a ticket for the s&ddling-paddock and
a field-glass, was rattling it into the capacious bag
of generous Mister Moses. How on earth the
other fellow manages it all—tha most people’s
Cup problem.

In a recent London Sketch appe several
blocks of Vic. native beauties, accompanied by
letterpress in which the Australian girl is summed
up. She, it would appear, is only happy when she
has got an Englishman to adore. She dotes on an
Englishman, and is morally prone in admiration
before an imparted idol with a cotton-waste chest
disguised under a long coat of immaculate fit,
large teeth, a glass jammed in its eye, and an ex-
pression of concentrated and unutterable “haw”,,
on its sheep-like countenance.

They are praying for rain in Adelaide
now, to the S. A. Joss. It is debated whether
the N.S.W, Joss can make rain inland, but cer-
tainly he is no use over the border. Evidently,
every province has its own providential rain-
maker. Federation of all the Josses, so that one
set of prayers will move the lot, is badly needed.
Just now there is a great waste of pious effort.

“Yes, father was connected with Mr.
Nolan’s church,” said the daughter of the poor,
hard-up, bailiff-tortured ex-soldier, who made a
sensation at North Sydney (North Sydney again !)
last week by cutting his own and his wife’s throat.
“We had prayers each morning. Father was
very good to the church, and taught a Bible-class
at the lecture-hall, where the Wesleyan classes
meet.” My son, paste this in your hat : The
road to mania lies through the gate of sincere,
religion. The imitation pietists—the sugar-sand-
ing grocer, the plate-passing elder who absconds
with the savings of the widow and orphan, these
are mentally as tough as leather. But the fervent
believer with the narrow head, and the protruding
eye, and the scanty hair, and the galvanic lurch—
he is safe only within the four walls of an asylum.

* * *
The new co-adjutor Bishop of Brisbane
is known within (and even outside) Anglican
Church circles in his native Victoria as “ Jack ”
Stretch. Ordained by circumstances to the
Church, and born with the gift of oratory, the
clean-cut prelate is “Jack” by nature. He is
one of the few clergymen who can tilt his hat
back from his brows and thrust his hands into his
trousers pockets without seeming to be a goat in
lambs’ clothing. Originally the curate at St.
Andrew's, Brighton (Melb.), young John Stretch’s
first rise was to the “living” of St. Mark’s, Fitzroy,
where he had the assistance of another sprightly
young parson, the Rev. E. S. Hughes, in giving a
cheerful turn to pious thought. From Fitzroy he
was called back to St. Andrew’s as vicar, and in
due course came a proposal to make Jack Stretch
an archdeacon of Ballarat. Warned by the
experience of his good, hard-working father, who
had never got beyond the archdeacon stage of
advancement, the Vicar of St. Mark’s stood out
for the vacant deanery, and carried his point.
And now, after a short term as Dean of Ballarat,
the Venerable Stretch, in the full vigor of his
early forties, is about to seek a warmer region
and fight the devil with a bishop’s staff. In pub-
lishing the photo, of this live Australian and
intellectual ornament to his profession, The Bul-
letin doesn’t wish to cast a suspicion upon his
religious character. It is true that he bears an
outward resemblance to a certain vice-regal
musician. But the inward spiritual difference
between Bishop Jack Stretch and ex-Governor
Bill Robinson is said to be all that the most devout
church attendant could desire. “ Jack ” Stretch
is the first Australian native, educated wholly in
Australia, to be made an Anglican bishop.
He got his Arts-degree at Melb. University,
and his theology at Trinity College, Melb. The
first Australian native to be made an Anglican
bishop was Bishop Parry, of Dover, who was
suffragan to the Archbishop of Canterbury.
Bishop Stretch, by the way, was formerly a clerk
in the Vic. Registrar General’s office, and had
nearly become a barrister,
* * *
The Dean case has now reached the “ re-
luctant ” stage. Everyone is sick of it, and wants
no share in its later development. Salomons,
Q.C., “ very reluctantly ” made his exposure.
Attorney-General Want wishes it “ at the bottom
of the sea,” and “ washes his hands of the whole
business,” relegating to someone else the duty of
finding a bill against the alleged conspirators.
Prosecutor Heydon accepted his brief “ very un-
willingly.” and would have been glad to have had
nothing to do with it. Several police-court wit-
nesses said their evidence was given against their
own inclination. Mr. Johnston, S.M., avers he
did not want the hearing of the case, and several
other minor persons are forced participators iy
the last act.

# * *
Cabman Sweetman, who drove Mary Jane
Hicks to the scene of the alleged Mt. Rennie out-
rage, is to be released Nov. 28, after serving nine
years of his 14 years’ sentence. The three lash-
ings prescribed by the Judge were medically
remitted, because of Sweetman’s ill-health—and,
within the last two years, a large piece of
prisoner’s skull was sawn out in the gaol hospital,
with a view to relieving paralysis, the result of an
old-time accident. The operation was successful.
By this release, the Reid Cabinet throws a tub to
the whale of public opinion, in the hope that
enquiry will now be burked concerning the manner
of the trial and the sentences of the remaining
prisoners.

A Ballarat man accused of tampering with
children was found guilty and—sentenced to five
years and doses of the cat at six months’ intervals?
No fear. He was a well-to-do citizen, and was
fined .£25. A fine covers a multitude of sins—in
the well-to-do citizen. The law holds that no
man should have vicious propensities who cannot
afford to pay for them, and it is only when
“ Justice ” cannot take it out of a man’s pocket
that she takes it out of his hide.

for fine instead of gaol, on the ground that
he was “ well-connected in the old country.”

A BEAUTIFUL COMPLEXION.
It has long’ been known to scientists that for
improving the appearance and beautifying the
face oatmeal is far and away superior to all other
foods, but it had one drawback—the tendency to
heat the blood. To overcome this defect the at-
tention of eminent scientists lias been devoted to
the removal of this imperfection ; and now, after
long years of careful and diligent research, art has
triumphed, and Uncle Toby’s Rolled Oats is the
result, for, by skilful manipulation in the manu-
facture, all the heating properties have been ex-
tracted, so that now a beautiful complexion of
peach and ivory may be had by anyone who makes
a.continuous use of Uncle Toby’s Rolled Oats.

• • •
The Bulletin’s review of “Gallia” recalls
a story of Melb. Coroner Youl’s about a young
couple who had no children, much as they desired
them. The husband called on him one day to ask
if there would be anything illegal in having his
wife’s sister “to supply the baby element.” The
Coroner asked, “What about your wife?” and
was staggered by the reply, “ Oh, it was she who
suggested it.” The Coroner then said he would
like to have a talk with these women, and
presently the would-be father, the wife, and the
sister came together and discussed the situation
with the doctor, and were delighted to find there
was no law against their proposed arrangement,
which was afterwards entered into.

# * #
Sweet are the uses of those vice-regal
levees. The other Monday a mob of Melbourne
men went up to Government House that they
might get several newspaper advts. out of shaking
hands with the new Governor. There are two
grades of social distinction in the hand-shaking
“privilege”—a public entree for the many and a
private entree for the few. Seedy individuals who
have been brought to smash by the failure of
Davies or Munro banks and tentacles, are practi-
cally warned off the premises. They don’t come
in anywhere at these demoralising functions.
Next morning, if they are not too poor to buy a
newspaper, they read (in large type) the names of
Mr, J. Munro and Mr. Matthew Davies in the list
of executive councilors who got at Brassey by
the aforesaid private entree. What a lesson in
social distinction for Democrats and busted share-
holders !
* s * *
Acting-Gov. Madden, of Victoria, got on
to the delicate subject of limbs the other day.
Before a large audience he stated that woman
should ride a bicycle, because the exercise helped
her “ to acquire that condition of roundness, firm-
ness, and plumpness in her body and limbs, which
will make her most attractive to the male sex.
That is all she wants, and all she looks for in the
glass.” This undisguised contempt for everything
except plumpness and limbs abolishes right off
any suspicion that J. Madden is a man of culture,
and reduces him in one act to the level of his
own waxed moustache, Astrachan-coat and
beautifully hair-oiled cranium.
* * *
Ex-Waxcelleucy John Madden played the
Governor to the fullest extent during his term of
stopgapship—and this in a way that no one has
done since British Australia was founded. In
imitation of Lord Hopetoun he even wrote letters
to the Railway Dept, and the police, thanking
them for their attention. Acting-Gov. Stawell
would have sickened at such vanity and display.
W.F.S. merelv took his screw whenever he acted;
and signed pap rs and did what he was told and
went back to the Supreme Court when the
Governor arrived. There is no authority or pre-
cedent for the capers of Madden.

A young Melbourne man and his best girl
were invited to a party at a house whose mistress
had lately increased the population. He naturally
asked his hostess about the baby, but the lady,
having been rendered somewhat deaf by a severe
cold, thought he was asking after the cold, not the
baby. Her answer was, “I usually have one
every winter, but this one’s the worst I ever had.
It keeps me awake at night a good deal, and at
first confined me to bed. I believe you yourself
are going to have one just like mine. Would yon

A marital dispute, lately before Vic. court,
was founded on the fact that a man who had
recently re-married returned home one day and
found that in his absence the undertaker had
delivered to his second wife a bill for the inter-
ment of the first. Verily it is best to be off with
the old love, &c.

The cadet remnant is all but cleared out
of the Vie. police force—gone for ever; and
Graham Berry ought never, in gratitude, to be
forgotten by the constabulary, for he choked a new
generation of Chornleys, Nicholsons and Sadliers.
Nearly 20 years ago, iStandish, Winch and Co.
prepared a scheme for the admission of new
cadets, which would have killed all the hopes of

the “rankers,” but Berry, as Premier, put it
behind the grate, and promoted six sergeants to
inspectorships. Thenceforward the game was up,
and soon the force will be all officered by rankers.
Montford was the first to get up, only because he
was a “gentleman ” —and captured Power.

THE SUNBEAM.
(Dedicated to Lady Brassey.)
The Sunbeam, glad chaser of murky night,
With glory of loveliness, life and light,
Mantling the dawn with each gorgeous hue,
Rivalling diamonds with drops of dew,
On pinnacles kissing the virgin snows
Into maiden blushes of bright red rose,
Recalling the flowers of a thousand dyes
To, in perfumed beauty, unclose tbeireyes,
Awakening the birds’ melodious song,
Beasts, forest and field, and the human throng,
Though sickness and sorrow to pain awake,
And through the grass glides the venomous snake,
Pain, sorrow and wrong are but slight alloy
Compared to the cheerfulness, health and joy
That come in the glow of the Sunbeam.
FOR THE LADIES WHICH GIVE ME MR DINNER.
As the sombre hues of Eve was falling
Upon a prisoner in his lonely cell,
The ifuard outside below the hour is calling
The time the mocking cry—alls well.
Ah me this irotiey fate tis hard to bear
As I offer up to heaven my heart-felt prayer.
And now to find my life for ever more
To be spent within four stone walls.
Tint there is the iron bound prisoner there,
,His life to be closed from this world in dispair,
And as he lies in his cell,
Ehut banished and forlorn,
iLike a limb from his country, his body is cast to be tom.
(Signed) G. Dean.
FAREWELL.
Can we recapture, or keep
At flood the spring-tides of the deep,
Or once again the happy dreams of sleep
Can we recapture?
Love’s bud unfolded, tender, full of trust —
Love’s rose blazed red. a sudden fire of lust—
Its petals, faded now. are in the dust
Q-
And gone the rapture.

The Wild-Cat Column .
The Viotomn Banking Commission hns tit last
presented Its report, and bo the disgust of the local
financiers, lb oordlally approves of the Slate Bank
principle. It proposes that the Bost-otfloe and the Com-
niisHi'iners Savings Hanks should he amalgamated and
should const ii ut« one department of the State Institution!.
The second department would he the note-issuing depart-
ment, The State Bank Is to take over the entire note'
Issue of the province, and its paper is to he redeemable
In gold at the Bank itself, hut Is to he legal tender every-
where else throughout Victoria. A gold reserve, equal Co
25 percent, of the issue, is to be kept on hand, which
should bs more than sufficient. The third department
will serve the purpose of a State Land Bank, and will
make advances on landed security at a low rate of in-
terest. It will raise money by the sale of bonds, and lend
out the proceeds at the lowest figure that will cover in-
terest and working expenses, and leave a margin for
possi 1)1 e losses. Tlie fourth department will manage the
Government and municipal accounts and the public debt,
float Government loans, make advances to municipalities,
and keep the current accounts of private cus-
tomers. A lot of the details of the scheme have
Still to lie worked out, hut so far as the out-
lines go the Vie. State Bank idea looks thoroughly
practical and workable. One notable point is that, by
combining the Savings Bank,. the note-issuing business,
the State land-bank, and the ordinary banking depart-
ment in one institution, an enormous saving will be
effected in working expenses. The savings business is at
present done by one Govt, institution and one semi-
private institution with a Govt, guarantee. The note-
issuing business is done by 10 separate hanks, each of
which must do its own printing, and keep its own gold
reserve, and pay its own management expenses. The
Land Bank department would do the work of several
hundred private and joint-stock financiers, each of whom
must make his own working expenses out of the miser-
able borrower. The amount saved in costs of manage-
ment alone, under anything like intelligent control,
should yield a very handsome profit.
If the State Bank of issue gets started, how-
ever, it may he safely assumed that the private institu-
tions will do their level best to keep its notes out of cir-
culation. They will have to accept them, but in place of
sending them out again they will pay their customers in
gold and demand cash from the State Bank for the notes.
Unless the State Bank has branches throughout the pro-
duce, which it won’t have—at least not for some time—it
can o-Iy circulate its notes extensively in the country
d st icts through the private banks, and the only way to
keep ih in afloat is either to let the private hanks make a
profit out of the business, or else to fall upon them wdth
an enactment by which a certain portion of their cash
reserves must be kept in State notes. The latter is the
surest way.
• • • •
The balance-sheet of the National Bank of Aus-
tralasia appears in another column of this issue. The
National, since the reconstruction of 1863, has increased
ns capital by £821,000, and has wiped off £1,650,000 of
deposits, and reduced its total liabilities by a little over
3,000,000. Also, it has coin, bullion, and liquid securities
on hand to the extent of 6s. 10|d. for every £1 of its
liabilities, which is a remarkably good position for its
creditors, considering thatflve-eighthsof thesaid liabilities
don’t begin to fall due until somewhere between 1898
and 1900. If the National would only take (he bold step
of releasing a great part of its fixed deposits, which it is
quite able to do, it would save a lotin interest and should
h-come once more a profitable concern for its share-
holders. At present the terms of the reconstruction
scheme make a considerable difficulty, as no provision
was made for compelling depositors to take their money
before the due date. Still there is every probability that,
if the release was made, a good many depositors would
take their money voluntarily, and thus ease the 4i per
cent, incubus.
• • • •
The shareholders of the Maoriland Colonial
Bank have finally ratified the agreement to sell out. their
institution to the B.N.Z. This transaction is another
commentary on the foolishness and hollowness of bank
b dance-sheets. The Colonial, according - to its published
statements, has £485,000 of capital and reserves
intact, and it realises six per cent, divs., which
are not unreasonably low in these times, and the
directors have stated over and over again that there is no
skeleton whatever in the background, and everything is
quite sound. When the B.N.Z. runs this business with
its own staff and premises it will save at least half the
expenses, and then the profits will he about 11 per cent.
Yet the Colonial gets nothing for the goodwill of this
splendid business, and is so glad to be rid of it that it
sacrifices a lot of the shareholders’ capital and sells out
much below' cost. In other words, it pays the B.N.Z. for
carting the darned thing away. How much faith have
the Colonial shareholders in their own balance-sheet
under these conditions? And what is the good of one
balance-sheet after another assorting that provision has
been made for " bad and doubtful debts ” when an over-
haul promptly reveals £300,000 of doubtful debts that
haven’t been provided for out of only £1,732 000 ad-
vances? ’
Still, the present generation of bankers isn’t
wholly to blame. The public always assumes now that
there is something concealed, and if the Colonial of
Maoriland had acknowledged its £300,000 of doubtful
debts, the public would have promptly assumed that
there were £300,000 worth more not acknowledged
Hohody in these days would ever credit a banker with
telling the whole truth.
• * * •
Re the balance-sheets of the Royal and Colonial
Banks (Melbourne). Both these institutions report that
their assets are so much, “ subject to the adjustment of
losses,” which are to be written off capital as soon as the
new Companies Act is passed. Neither states what the
losses amount to, therefore the amount of the assets is an
unknown quantity. And the auditors, who certify to
the correctness of the balance-sheets, practically certify
that the assets are an unknown quantity. The
proper wav, under the circumstances, is to debit profit
and loss with the amount of the losses, and then certify
to something tangible. J
The London Bank of Australia,, it is cabled
has finished the half-year with a net profit of £7082 Lasl
half year the profit was £8216; the one before the hank
realised £7026, and for the half ending 31st December
1893, the profit was £5986, so for the first time since the
reconstruction there is a serious falling-off. Hitherto
each balance-sheet has shown a decided improvement on
the one before. As usual, the preference shareholders
get their 51 per cent, dividend, absorbing £4721, and the
balance goes to profit god loss account, which has now a
credit balance of £10,222. The ordinary shareholders are
still out in the cold* and as it would take £31,400 (or 41
times the total earnings) to pay a half-year’s div. at the
rate of 5 per. cent, per annum on the ordinary capital
their prospects at present look exceedingly hopeless. The-
earnings, afier tin- pr ference shareholders have had
(heir l,h. of flpsh, nr - at the rate of rather less than Bs.
per cent- on the ordinary capital. The London
Bank has reduced its fixed deposits by £6OO 000'
in. Ihe last 1;8 months, and the reconstructed money is
now only £4,254,000. It has also the very large sum of
£1,654.000 in cash, equal to about 26 per cen f . of its lia-

right, to,pay off its deposits at six months’
notice, it seems surprising, now that it is ballasted down,
tyith s.') much unprofitable corn, that it doesn’t start the
releasing process on a large scale.

The municipal fathers of Balmain (Sydney) want
to (ret an Aot authorising them to raise money by a large,
robust mortgage on the park and town-ball —as if any
mortgagee wanted a town-hall left on his hands. Balmain
is a dusty, unkempt, negleoted suburb, where nothing is
certain except that you will meet the night-cart on your
way home, and it consists mostly of debt and back-lane
and infuriated alderman and precipice and lost- dog and
Old ash-barrel.

The estate of the deceased Thomas Manifold, of
Oamperdown (Vie.), paid the Treasury £14,374 in legacy
duty. Tide is the sort of thing which makes the hard-up
Treasurer watch the local Fat Mao with a ghoulish eye,
and wonder if there is any chance of his death within the
present financial year. How can any Treasurer be a
Christian when there is only a week left of the financial
year, and £lOO,OOO deficit looming, and a millionaire
slowly dying of dropsy in the next street?

* • ♦ •
It is sheer brutality to legislate (as in N.S.W.
Public Service Bill) that, because civil-servants cannot be
garnisheed, therefore they can be "sacked” summarily for
an unsatisfied petty debts judgment. Dismissal may mean
the loss of hundreds of pounds nut of payments made
during many years to the Superannuation' Fund. The
true democratic step is to abolish garnishee orders
altogether, as has been done in England Nay, the ideal
democratic step is to abolish all processes of law for the
recovery of debts. That would bring about a grand
revival of “ spot cash ” and trustworthy “ honor.”
» ♦ » *
“ Square the liquidator ” is the lay down South
whenever squeezing is attempted for payment of calls in
connection with a deceased building or financial co.
In many cases, the liquidator’s chief occupation is in
coming to terms with those debtors who have anything to
part, in order to avoid the chance of getting nothing for
himself.

The British Australasian draws attention to
the fact that whilst Colonel Gerard Smith is going out as
Governor of Westralia, a Mr. H. Gerald Smith figures as
secretary of a late Westraliau venture.

J. H. Butt, formerly accountant of the A.J.S.
Bank’s London branch, and subsequently manager of the
defunct, Federal, who, some time since, was lucky enough
to receive the appointment of secretary to J. B. Robinson,
the South African millionaire (many times over), has now
been made manager-in-chief (at a very big salary) of
Robinson’s Bank Ltd., a concern lately started by Robin-
son aforesaid.

Two more N.S.W. solicitors struck off the rolls
last week, and cases against others are pending. One
shark confessed to receiving a client’s money and "appro-
priating it to his own use.” Regarding the other, it was
said by the Court that " there was evidenc°, and string
evidence, that he had fraudulently misappropriated the
money.” After a Supreme Court Judge had used lan-
guage like this, the criminal prosecution of any ordinary
man would follow automatically.

Cabled from London :—“ A panic on Mr.
Barnato’s hank was averted by the fact that the Lord
Mayor of London recently entertained him at dinner, and
his buying shares of the bank led to a rush for them on
the part of the public.” Woe to the Australian banker
whom an Australian mayor should ostentatiously ask to
dinner—if the depositors got to know it!
• * * •
Vic. Insolvency Judge Molesworth sat down
heavily the other day on the typical assignee. This person
was realising on an estate, the principal item being the
equity of redemption of 1441 acres of land. The property
realised £2882, but. after paying the mortgagee, the
balance was only £245. The assignee, however, charged
6 per cent, commission on the mortgage money, which
didn’t belong to the estate, as well as on the £245 which
did, and thereby mopped up all the little property.
Judge Molesworth, however, fixed the remuneration at
£23 instead of the £152 claimed, and thus another hard
knock was given to the insolvency trustee business in
Melbourne.

Dear Bulletin, —I applied, the other day, for
the advertised secretaryship of a limited liability co. Two
days later I received a type-written note as subjoined
from an utter stranger, who could not have known my
address except through the application referred to :
“ Hear Sir,—l take the opportunity of advising you that
I have for sale 229 shares in the (Blank, Etcetera) Co.”
the co. whose advt. I answered—“paid up to £3 per
Share. They can be had for the nominal sum of Id. (one
penny) each. The price is very low-, and as the co. has
large and valuable assets, they are worth your attention
as a speculation. The sale to he subject to immediate
transfer. If you are not disposed to take the lot, let me
know promptly how many you will purchase, and oblige.
Yours faithfully (Sharebroker).” How’s that for high?
I may mention there’s a big contingent liability on the
shares. Ought I to reply and offer to take over the transfer
in consideration of receiving £lO ?—Yours, X.

♦ * * •
Among Broken Hill miners pneumonia is far
more deadly than ever typhoid was in the early days of
the town, when most of the houses were built against
filth-tips. There is often a difference of 35 to 40 deg.
between the temperatures in working face and drive or
shaft ; and while it is often from 90 to 120 deg. in the
face, it is rarely more than 65 at midnight on the surface,
even in the s-unmer.

Thr Central North Co. (no liability) liquidates
—an illustration of what The Bulletin was saying the
other week about the No Li ability Act and the need for
insisting o i more actually paid-up capital hieing available
for prospecting purposes.
* * ♦ »
A few years ago the silver-lead (mostly lead)
mines,of Kansas and Missouri got down ion to zincblende,
as the Broken Mill mines have done. They set to work
to make use of the zinc, ;md now get out about 150,000
to is of ore annually, yielding anything ,pp to 30,000 tons
of zinc. The o:e before being marketed is concentrated,
and then carries an average of about ’65 per cent, of
metal.

811. Proprietary, ii now one of “ masted y inactivity.”
In other words, they are letting the thing B urn itself out,
O.ipt \\ nrren and the lood fire brigade people deserve
muoh more than thanks for the way In which they beat
the flames out of Block 10. Of course, the latter is not

* * ♦ ♦
The Ashcroft sulphide process looks like boom-
ing in London. Anyhow, it has already had the effect of
booming the principal Barrier mines. Lots of things are
beginning to dawn on Australian shareholders—amongst
others, why a long option was secured on the Central.
But there is some consolation in the fact that ±175,000 in
hand is worth fully 1250,000 (at which the mine, it is
said, will be re-sold) in the solution of the sulphide pro-
blem. That problem is not yet solved. It is like Federa-
tion—it is in the “ hair,” and may get J. Bull by the
wool.

The Hoffnung crowd appears to be responsible
for the launching of the Gibraltar Consolidated G.M. Co.,
which has been formed to work the old mine of that name
at Adelong. The most flattering calculations as to pros-
pective profits have been published, which, if correct,
make one wonder how it is that such a wonderful pro-
perty was not floated in Sydney. You people down
under, remarks Tub Bulletin’s London correspondent,
don’t seem to know when you have a good thing at your
very doors. A net profit of nearly €llO,OOO is not to he
sneezed at, and to think in such bad times of such a sum
being allowed to leave the country to be pocketed in
England!

* * * •
Coolgardie news is remarkably inconsistent
with the falling prices of the stocks. There are abso-
lutely no dealings in Rayley’s, and Plunderderrys are un-
saleable in London, where there are no enquiries for the
Wealth of Expectations. Golden Bars, after that long-
deferred crushing, have dropped £1 ; Moon’s (one of the
mining tipster’s favorites) looked at one time like “ reach-
ing par”—and now' look a good deal more like falling to
a humble “bob.” Maritana—that marvellous mine
which (if we had believed the Coolgardie wire-workers)
lots of people in London wanted to buy for £90,000, has
now about half that market-value. Maritana North shares
can be got at £1 a dozen. Croesus Extended—another of
the frequently “ tipped ” mines—are less than half the
(market) value of a month ago. After carrying up-
wards the stocks of its surrounding mines, Boidder has
now to suffer for their collapse. That mine is said to he
richer to-day than ever, but its shares are coming down.
• * * *
In their frantic efforts to keep up the Westra-
iian gold-boom the wire-workers say some funny tilings:
“Kalgoorlie.—Warden Fielding is allowing residence-
areas to be pegged out by the holders of miners’ rights.
This will enable miners to build homes for themselves,
instead of sending the bulk of their earnings to the
eastern colonies for the support of their families.” First
time we have heard of any difficulty of this kind on W.A.
goldfields.
• * ♦ •
London market, after staggering under its enor-
mous load of Westralian “wild cats.” has practically
collapsed. The writer’s last London letter indicated that
the market was getting top-heavy and could not stand
the strain of “wild cat” mongering much longer, and
now comes a cable saying the market has burst. It will
take mines of proved value, but the cats of which nine-
tenths of the W.A. mines floated in London consist are
“ not wanted.” In sympathy with J. Bull’s refusal to
carry any more “ wild-cats,” Coolgardie stocks are
knocked endw’ays.
The mining tipster is again in the soup.
After advising (a month ago) the purchase of certain
Coolgardie stocks at about double their present value, he
says, “ Looking at the West Australian gold market we
think that fear and fright must now have had their day.
The fall had next to nothing to do with the mines them-
selves. They are as good as ever.” Worse hick—they
are as bad as ever. As the losses of the ill-advised buyers
represent money made by others, those who sold out at
top prices in Coolgardie mines as Moons, Golden Bar,
Croesus Extended, &c., should show their appreciation
by banqueting that mining tipster.

• * * •
A Coolgardie wire : “ Mr. Gerard Stockfeld
starts next week for Africa to report on gold-concessions
in Matabeleland for an English co. If his report is not
satisfactory the co. will transfer its operations to Kalgoor-
lie.” Stockfeld is one of the few Coolgardie mining
examiners whose reports stand the test of time. He has
been there over two years and has condemned more
“ shows” called mines than perhaps any other man on the
field. Had he joined the powerful army of boomers he
would have lost reputation and made more money.
* * * *
The latest Coolgardie “strike” is that of the
telegraph operators, who demand a minimum wage of
£2OO a year, and are going to stop all wires if they
don’t get it. As the wires are the secret of Coolgardie’s
greatness they will probably win. Imagine Coolgardie a
month in telegraphic darkness! It would die of the
shock.
Another Coolgardie “strike”—this time eight
miles from Siberia. The prospectors nap stone on a
poor-looking reef, finally strike it rich, come into town
with 400 z., and “(he reef is still rich at 3ft. deep.” Of
course, the mine is under offer, and the chances are no
more work will be done till it is sold. When the world
realises that a three-foot hole isn’t a mine and that in 99
cases out of 100 it never becomes one, and that the
patch mines of Coolgardie, including the boss of them
(the Plunderderry), all wither at the sight of a piok, Cool
gardie will have a less easy time,

From Coolgardie—and from an experienced
man trained at Freiburg:—“Ooolgardie is booming fast,
but it won't last long. Before Xmas everything will be
quiet again. Next month a lot of sharks will leave this
hot place and take a holiday trip—some to England,
M.L., and Tas. Then there will be a six weeks’ exemp
tion, as usual, without any reason this year, and a lot ol
miners, laborers, and others will have to go to Perth and
Albany, followed by storekeepers and business-men, and
Coolgardie will be, for a couple of months, a deserted
place. The railway will bring back a lot of the grabsters,
but they won’t again boom the place. Sept, and Oct.
have seen the flotation of numerous leases on the strength
ot their being within telescopic sight of the Great Boul-
der, Brown Hill, or some other bigconoern. Some others
have been floated on having the same formation, or the
same vegetation, or the same kind ot lizards running
over the properly, and by ‘ skilful management, &c.,
I have no hesitation, &c., in recommending the purchase
of Leases No, . I have the honor, &c., John Joiinby,
M.A.M.E.Y.X.Z. (Receipt for £lOO enclosed.’) In six
months we will see the results of this sort of thing. Five
or six mines will certainly pay divs., a few more will pay
expenses, and by this time next year we will buy a 10-
head battery in Coolgardie as cheap as an old ’bus in Mel-
bourne.”

* * * *
From the way in which Croesus Extended is
being boomed in Sydney—a Sunday paper has no less
than four blatant direct puffs in one issue—it might be
thought that a fortune was going begging in this stock.
The Bulletin has reliable information from the spot that
(to put the matter mildly) there is absolutely nothing in
the concern to make a fuss about. Ditto re the Nymagee
copper mine, which is also being most industriously
boomed. This for the benefit of investors; mere stock-
choppers may be left to take care of themselves.

Cue Stock Exchange wires The Bulletin com-
plaining that the eastern press doesn’t boost up the Mur-
chison as that field deserves, and stating that last month’s
export of gold was 10,5500 z., not counting many parcels
taken away privately. Also, the Kinsella battery has
started with splendid prospects to crush from a reef now
opened up fcr nearly 1000 ft. ; and over 50oz. were picked
up the other day after one shot in the Westralian mine,
Cuddingwarra.
When the secretary of One Stock Exchange was
instruct d te send that wire, he was Hot told to say that
Uie Murchison was without one div.-paying mining co ,
and that the gold output from a field of enormousexlent,
with a great number of mines and plenty of water and
crushing-power, is not equal to Mt. Morgan and is in-
sufficient to pay expenses.
Not one, hut two mines on the Murchison last
week “ each dislodged 50oz. of gold with a single shot.”
That is average W. A. gold-mining to a nicety— 50oz. for
one shot. After that, 50 shots for one oz.
♦ * * *
H. C. Callahan, who came to Australia early
last year with Bratnober to travel over the Westralian
goldfields, left Sydney this week for London, en route for
South Africa, where he goes under engagement at a
princely salary. This will be good news in W.A., particu-
larly on theMurohison, where the fact that Callahan was
still in Australia, and might again come to report on
Murchison mines, wasa hideous nightmare to somepeople.
He was a cold, brutal truth-teller, and a position wtiich
any W.A. mining man would envy is his rewaid.

the strength of the Great Boulder or the Londonderry ?
or was it because some of the pioneer development com-
panies managed to pay good divs. out of the profits made
on sales of properties which may themselves never pay a
fraction to the shareholders?

* * • *
At Beechworth (Vic.) last week Palmer and
Skidmore, for six weeks’ pick-work, won 68oz. 17dwt.
from 12 tons from their Sir Wilfred Lawson reef. Going
on for three years the pair have picked away at their
claim for crushings never going under the ounce. This
year their profit will stand them about £l5OO each.

• • ♦ «
W. R. Wilson won the V.R.O. Derby and D.
James the Melh. Cup. Broken Hill enabled both to go
horse-racing. Wilson, In the very early days of the
Barrier field, was general manager of the Barrier Ranges
Prospecting Association, which owned the Day Dream ;
and afterwards, as a director of the B.H. Proprietary, he
visited America and led to the importation of Schlapp
and Patton. Before Patton came, his brother, S. R. Wil-
son, was general manager. James was a Kapunda (S.A.)
teamster, who happened to he in the district dam-sinking
(he had come up with loads) when Charley Rasp p°gged-
out the Broken Hill. Rasp was then camped at the Nine
Mile, and that was where James’s contract was. Thus the
Kapunda man "got in,” and, luckily, didn’t try hard
enough to get out. The papers that describe him as “ a
successful speculator in Broken Hill scrip,” don’t know.
James, by the way, like pretty nearly all the others, has
done nothing much for Broken Hill’s charitable institu-
tions, which are sorely pressed by the scores of men
whom the mines disable.

Politician story. A famous writer went to
dine with a veteran politician, and sat at table
alongside the politician’s daughter. “ Pa’s going
to get married again,” she whispered. “And
who, may I enquire,” asked the author, “is the
forfunate young lady?” “There she is,” said
the daughter, “ handing round the cabbage.” The
politician was marrying his servant.
William George Garrard,
with his breast plastered over
with war-medals, and at the
head of a ragged and hungry
following, was long a promin-
ent figure in Auckland (M.L.)
unemployed agitations, and
contrived to make things so
lively that whenever a public
man caught a glimpse of the
medals, he used to dodge up
the nearest right-of-way. But
a wily politician muzzled Gar-
rard with the dignified billet of
curator of collarless dogs, and
thenceforth William George
disappeared from the political
arena, to wield the lassoo among
vagrant mongrels. But the
dogs have got to know W.G.,
and when they can’t sport the
legal badge of respectability,
they slink away from him guilt-
stricken, with their tails be-
tween their legs. Thus do
canine intelligence and dog-
conscience circumvent the
efforts of the patriot who sacri-
fices himself on the altar of a
civic billet.
Gov. Brassey once went
as part of an Imperial fad ora-
tion league to Gladstone at
Hawarden. Brassey somehow
got the opening speech to do,
and he did it. He had the
usual mass of statistics and
valuable information collected
by his secretaries. For this
occasion he was more dull and
honestly solid and stolid than
ever. When 15min. of the
speech had dropped with a
sickening thud into the Past, it
dawned on 37 men that one man
would not be missed from such
a crowd. In another five
minutes Gladstone and Brassey
were the sole occupantsof the
big room, and each one of the
deserters was indignant in the
garden at 36 men who had left
Gladstone at the mercy of
Brassey’s eloquence. The indignation took 40min.
or so to cool down, and then the crowd returned
quietly to hear the last 25min. of Brassey’s speech.
The wholeheartedness of the welcome ex-
tended to Gov. Brassey by Vic. Ms.P. may be
judged from the fact that one member, on the day
following his brother’s death, and while the
latter’s body was still unburied, went down the
Bay in the Aramac to meet H.E. and joined in
the festivities.
At Hill End (N.S.W.), recently, a “re-
spected ” Teuton J.P. was adjudicating in a
poultry-stealing case, and apparently thought it
imperative that he should ask every witness some
question or other. One of his interrogations :
“INow, tell me dis; de leedle vite turkey, vas he
speckled or vas he grey ?”
Sample of S.A. Gov. Buxton as a public
speaker; “ Mistah Chahman. It—ah—gives me
much pleashah to be prahsent to dahy. When
fahst ah came to South Australiah ah was greeted
vahry nahsly by the little —ah—childrahn (smile
and interval for applause) and now ah have
pleashah in meeting—ah—thah eldahs —ah,” &c.
There died in Maoriland, the other day,
Heremom Ratitapu, a centenarian. He was in
the prime of life when the first European settlers
came to the Clutha, and he used to relate that,
years ago, when tracking his canoe along the
shallows in the Kaihihu River, he picked up
nuggets which he cast away into the water as
useless.
The times are gone when on one hot day
Justice Redmond Barry called Joseph Henry
Dunne to order in open court for having his wig
off and lying before him on the bar table. Now,
Justices Hodges and Hartley Williams are often
seen with the horsehair beside their notebooks;
and Judge Hickman Molesworth sits for days at
a time without wig or gown.
Gov. Brassey has a queer, little, waddling
walk, reminding one of Elton as the vulgar specu-
lator in “A Bunch of Violets.” He waves a few
inches at a time, making a curious lurch forward
from the hips with every step he takes. It is evi-
dent that he is childishly vain of appearing in
uniform, with a sword dangling at his side, and a
big star pinned on to his coat.
The era of Scotch Governors has suddenly
ended in Australia ; the new Governors are Eng-
lish to a man. It seems but yesterday that there
was an odor of whisky and haggis in every Gov-
mentouse ; now there isn’t an Excellency left who
could wear a kilt without looking as if his knees
were a hidden crime that had suddenly come to
light.
Spagnoletti : “ After all, Parkes is wise.
Rev. Dr. Bromby, in Melb., at 75, took his 18-
year-old wife, who piloted him comfortably into
the haven at 80, and he was happy as the day
was long, instead of having grumbling frumps
around him. Bishop Moorhouse, with conspicu-
ous ignorance of human nature, and ineffable con-
ceit, thought he had laughed Bromby out of it.”
“Betrayal of Trust” is the title of an
article by “ Colonus ” in Auckland (M.L.)
Hebald. Discussing (apropos the Dean case) the
duty of a lawyer in relation to a confession of
guilt made to him by a client, “ Colonus ” says :
“The confession has been committed in trust,
and is there any honest reason that that trust
ought to be betrayed ? The confession is a pro-
perty, and is not the lawyer’s. It is only his in
trust. If he uses it for his own purposes, or for
anybody else’s purposes, he is a traitor.”
Premier Turner seemed inexpressibly un-
happy at the Brassey reception show-functions.
On the occasion of the presentation of the address
from the Leg. Assembly, Turner, in a dress-suit,
looked most miserable. The Governor turned to
him after reading his reply, and said, “ I know
you dislike these formal things, so I out it as
short as I could.” “ Thanks,” growled Turner.
Rumored in London, when the mail left,
that Premier Dick Seddon has his eye on the
M.L. Agent-Generalship, and that Ward, the
present Treasurer, is to succeed him as Premier,
The Agents-General, by the way, are themselves
a striking example of the Democratic character of
Australasian political and social preferment. One
is an ex-gardener, another an ex-miner ; Seddon,
as everybody knows, was formerly a publican.

Another veteran Sydney teacher has died
in the person of R. J. Horniman, who arrived
in N.S.W. in 1838 as assistant-surgeon of the ship
Palmyra. He was in one of the earliest parties
which followed Major Mitchell’s overland track
from Bathurst to Melbourne, and he subsequently
became a tutor under Dr. Lang at the Australian
College in Jamieson-street (1842), subsequently
undertaking the sole control of the College. He
was also connected with W. T. Cape, by whom
many leading Australians were educated. Until
some eight years ago Mr. Horniman continued
teaching. Amongst his pupils he numbered Chief
Justice Griffith (Q.). The veteran was 83 when
he died.

PEART, the champion high-diver.
Pastor Fitchett; “ Lord Brassey possesses
one accomplishment unknown to any of his pre-
decessors except, perhaps, Sir Charles Hotham
—he is a practical seaman and holds a master
mariner’s certificate.” There is no “ perhaps ”
about it. Hotham was a fine sailor, and had dis-
tinguished himself abroad before he came to Aus-
tralia. He did great things in the Parana River
against General Rosas, and was the first naval
officer to fight a ship under steam-power.
T. P. O’Connor on Huxley’s courtship :
When the Rattlesnake was in Sydney Harbour the
officers, young Huxley among the number, were invited
to a ball. There, for the first time, he met his future
wife, whose parents resided at Sydney. A few days after
they were engaged, and the ship sailed for Torres Straits
to complete the survey of the north coast of Australia,,
all communication being cut off for months at a time, and
then she returned direct to England. After that brief
acquaintance (not longer than a fortnight) it was seven
years before the lovers saw one another. At the end of
this time, on Huxley's appointment to the School of
Mines, he was in a position to claim his bride and welcome
her to their first home in St. John’s Wood. Huxley’s
love at first sight, and constancy during those seven long
years of separation, was richly rewarded, for it is im-
possible to imagine a pair more thoroughly suited.
Major Te Wheoro, one of the most re-
markable characters in Maori history, is dead. He
fought for the whites against the Maoris ; when
the war was over he went back and lived (as a
Government spy) with the King natives. When
attending Parliament during the session he lived
royally in pakeha fashion; during recess for a
month or so he dwelt tn a whare d la Maori,
sleeping on a mat, eating kumara, and wearing a
blanket, all the time keeping the Minister of the
day well posted in what was going on. Te
Wheoro accompanied King Tawhiao on his
memorable visit to England—when the Imperial
authorities treated the head of a gallant but un-
fortunate race with less consideration than was
extended to the monkeys sent as a present to
Queen Victoria by some African savage or other,
for the Queen received the monkeys but declined
to see Tawhiao. Te Wheoro’s career ended in
the only way possible—-he fell between two stools,
The Maoris spurned him as a traitor, the Buro-
P e f? 8 f. s a despicable, worn-out tool. He pro-
bably died poor, after all his knavery,
Purves, Q.C., won £lOOO clear over
Auraria’s Melb. Cup victory. He was, he said,
quite unfit for work the following day, and asked
the Bull Court to adjourn because he felt too ill to

.. Minnie,” a patient in Beechworth, Vie.,
Lunatic Asylum, has had “a career.” She
is a tall, beautifully - formed woman whose
Chinese husband had to put her in safe
keeping on account of periodical violent
attacks. Standing with her hand resting grace-
fully on the back of a chair she answered a visitor
who asked her “Why did you run away from
home as a girl?” “It’s very rude to ask ques-
fcions.” Then her haughtiness changed suddenly
to weeping, and with tears and wringing of hands
she began to sing, with perfect accent, “Plaisirs
d’Amour.” All that is known of her is that she
is the daughter of a church dignitary in England,
and her name is now Mrs. Wun Lung, or Ah Sin.
From English deanery to Chinese camp.
Peart, the diver of Fitz-
geralds’ show, is a cockney, 25
years old, sft. sin., and 9£st.
His father was a clever trapeze
artist, his grandfather a tum-
bler. He learned swimming
when 14, and practised diving
—never higher than 25 feet,
however, off the banks and trees
of the river Lee. At 16 he was
a public water-triokster—eat-
ing, drinking, talking, smoking,
writing, &c., under water. At
18 he pitted himself against
Finney, the human fish, who
stayed under water five minutes.
Peart stayed minutes, and
was pulled up insensible, with
blood flowing from his nose. In
’B6 lie beat Johnson with a dive
of 80 feet; in ’BB Jules Ferres
with 95 feet—Ferres cried like
a child over it. ’9O, at Johan-
nesburg, marked a new record
—llO feet, and in that year he
exhibited before the late Czar
and Czarina. In ’9l he aston-
ished the Austrians, in ’92 the
Danes and Belgians. In ’93 he
surprised the whole world with
a champion dive of 125 feet,
and then surprised himself, at
Antwerp’s ’93 Exhibition, with
a new dive of 137 feet. In
’94, the Tyne-men, seeing him
come 187 feet from the high level
bridge, reckoned him a corpse,
but were disappointed. And
after this London came each
night to the Royal Aquarium
to see Peart manage his tank-
dive there or miss it and be
mopped up with a sponge. The
corpse duly arrived—supplied
by his rival, Fuller, who leapt
from Tower Bridge, 227 feet, and
disappeared in the river for a
month after. Perhaps a million
people saw Peart flash through
that same 227 feet and bob up
with his head streaming blood
—he had struck a small piece of
driftwood. He went but six
feet below the water’s surface in
the great dive! Up at the
giddy height whence Peart dives not one man in a
hundred has the nerve to stand, and the daring
dive itself is into a tank—a coffin, really,
which must be always tricked out of its corpse.
But Peart likes it. He takes his two deep breaths
while the farthest listener hears him—so deathly
is the crowd’s bush. Then he springs out and up
—hovers ever so momentarily, gauges, dives. A
sweep of right or left hand carries him 12 feet left
or right; coming from 150 feet he can strike any
point in a 30-feet radius circle. Thus he steers
himself though the air and smiles confidently while
women faint. Death had him fairly twice and
missed him. Once at the Royal Aquarium when,
80 feet above the tank, Peart found himself 25 feet
horizontally outside of it. But he steered in some-
how. At Capt. Boynton’s ’94 Water Show the
ropes of Peart’s platform broke, letting the
diver down feet-first a sudden 80 feet. He
shrieked that time, for he couldn’t turn to
steer himself into the tank, but he managed
it somehow, hitting the water with his left
side and face, bruising himself terribly.
In Melbourne, where he dived 137 ft. and put his
platform higher when the authorities ordered him
lower, he hurt his stomach through the bag going
wrong in the Monte Oristo feat, and spat up
blood. At Adelaide he won a .£2O wager from a
soulless wretch who betted that he could tie the
bag so that Peart couldn’t get out. But Peart
undid the four knots and pocketed the cash.
Peart always closes his eyes entering the water —
the impact would damage them. And hitherto he
has (so he says) closed his eyes to many offers of
marriage—two from titled ladies. But he swears
that he will take the next good offer. Many
people have written to him asking him not to dive,
and in London a representative of the County
Council used to hang round and officially see that
Peart’s arrangements were right. Peart has given
£lOOO to London hospitals. He is a teetotaller
and cigarette smoker. He will dive any man in
Australia 65ft. into three feet depth of water.


ADVERTISEMENTS ON POST-
CARDS.
tO THE EDITOR OP THE AROUB.
Sir—Will yon kindly afford me a short
space in your columns in which I may make
my public protest against the newly-issued
postal-cards.
Again, I am' not a total abstainer,
duty obliges me to send a la'ge number of
short memos, and instructions to the young
clergy-and readers of my archdeaconry, and
I have hitherto freely dsed postal-cards for
this purpose, but it certainly sieems to me
altogether unseemly that while I send these
young men good advice on one side of the
card, I shall be compelled in future to re-
commend them to .drink “lager beer” on
the bther. —Yours,
JOHN E. HERRING.

Speaking at the pastor’s anniversary meet-
at the Burton-street Tabernacle on Thursday
evening, the president of the Baptist Union
(Mr. Hugh Dixson) enjoined the people to
be more practical in expressing their appre-
ciation of their minister. Persons who kept
fowls should remind him of their existence
by occasionally sending along a dozen of
eggs, or, if they reared pigs, a piece of pork
would answer the same purpose.

POLITIEAL POINTS
The N.S. W. Labor-party met Davitt sec-
retly, but seemed afraid to recognise him openly.
Why ?
Just-ice Minister Gould has just liberated
from prison three hundred—trout. As fish-
blooded animals they probably had his official
sympathy.
Member Price’s motion re Justice Win-
deyer still stands on N.S.W. Assembly notice-
paper. If Price is worth his salt he will press it
to a division even if he has to stand alone in that
division.
The Australian Parliament that passes a
Bill to abolish the political portfolio of the Attor-
ney-General, and to make that officer a civil-ser-
vant, permanent under all political changes, and
M hard to remove [almost) as a Judge, will be a
Parliament of deliverance, and the reformer who
beads the assaHt that leads to such a blessed
change will be be tWasbington of his political
sphere.

“ CUMJAM,” the aboriginal now awaiting
trial in Normanton gaol for the murder of
slation-manager Ferguson.

Patterson’s shoes as leader of Vie. Opposition.
The other Mack, M‘Kenzie, is the best man, but
he refused the honor. MTntyre’s leadership is a
guarantee of weakness.

Workman —following the lead of the shandygaff
M‘Gowen, M.L.A.—was absolutely the only
paper in Australia to approve the liberation of the
criminal solicitor Rofe under the First Offenders’
Act.
N.S. Wales temporary Crown-Prosecutor
Heydon (M.L.0.); “In consequence of the Attor-
ney-General being a politician and a member of
the Ministry, and as attacks had been made
on him, there certainly is a danger that the
administration of justice might be brought under
suspicion, if,’’ &e. No, sir. No danger that it
might be, and no “if ” about it. It has heea, and
it is.
