is acting as Q. Agent-General. Not a word yet
of how Garrick came to resign. Perhaps the
Govt, organs thought it too like political vengeance
to cast a pleasant hue over the dying days of
Boodlewraith. Rumored that the Ministry hesi-
tated long between MTlwraith and a lay figure
from Madam'- Tu-saud’s, but loyalty to their old
chief won the day.
Queensland some time ago imposed a half-
penny postage on newspapers upon the understand-
ing that N.S.W. would follow suit. N.S.W. has
not yet followed suit, and is losing, say, £40,000
revenue annually by the tact. The daily papers
own the N.S.W. Govt., body and soul. Besides,
free postage means the free circulation of hundreds
of shoddy organs masquerading as newspapers,
and in N.S.W. Shoddy is king.
Up to date, Morehead, M.P. (Q.), is the
last consistent Freetrader to hit his gimcrack Joss
on the point of the jaw—for this occasion only.
He wants an export-duty on wool going South to
encourage local sales. And, like them all, he
trotted out Mill’s musty dictum in favor of a
leetle Protection for new countries. Freetraders
can get too much of a good thing. Morehead, of
course, is in the wool-line himself.
In his enthusiasm for Freetrade, Jawbone
Neild, M.P., was last week led into stating that
the N.S.W. people had declared inferentially
against Australian Federation. Jawbone’s argu-
ment was that the N.S.W. voters had twice asked
emphatically for Freetrade; whereas Australian
Federation meant “ interprovinoial Freetrade
and protection against the world.” The last
clause is true. But the voters have not asked
emphatically for Freetrade. The Labor members
have permitted Freetrade assumptions by sinking
individually their fiscal opinions. Neild’s logic
should enlighten them.
The curse of Government by Contradic-
tion has fallen upon the last place which had
escaped it—Castlemaine (Vic.), the constituency
of the late J. B. Patterson. Hitherto, however
tangled the issues before the country at large,
Castlemaine has had only one distinct, unmis-
takeable point to decide. Patterson or no Pat-
terson, that was the point upon which every
Castlemaine election bad been worked for years.
The beautiful jumble which constitutes modern
politics had no influence whatever upon it. Now,
Castlemaine will be as ignorant as any other con-
stituency of what it is voting for.

The Maoriland Cri-
minal Court of Appeal
broke down badly at
almost the first attempt.
The law by which it was
constituted, it is dis-
covered, allows it to grant
a new trial only if the
Judges are convinced
that the original verdict
was against the weight
of evidence. The first
case of great public inter-
est which came before it
was that of the alleged
murderer, Louis Chemis,
who applied for a new
trial on the ground that
some valuable evidence
had come to light since
his conviction, and the
Court decided that it had
nothing to do with new
evidence, and dismissed
the applicition. The
Court of Appeal thus
doesn't promise to be of
much value. It can’t
grant a fresh trial because
new facts have come to
light, or because evidence
was i m properly ad ini tied,
or on account of mis-
direction by the Judge,
or because the witnesses
for the prosecution prove
to be persons of bad
character, or because of
perjury. It must stick
to the one question as to
the weight of evidence,
and as evidence isn’t coal
to be weighed by the ton
even that is a matter
wherein its usefulness
will depend al most wholly
on the Judge’s liver.
The S.A. Govt, is making the abolition
of capital punishment an open question. Premier
Kingston and Education-Minister Cookburn are
against the death-penalty, but their colleagues are
in favor of its retention.
From Maoriland, Victoria, N.S. Wales
and S.A. come the same wail. The Parliamentary
session is either ended or getting near its end, and
hardly anything has been done, and half the
measures which represent the labor of the session
must be thrown overboard unfinished from sheer
weariness, to be started all over again next session.
Why doesn’t some plain, sensible statesman intro-
duce the plan of taking up the unfinished bills
next session where they were left off ? The plan
of starting everything afresh would apply well
enough in a new Parliament, but when the Par-

Treas. Holder (S.A.) proposes to square
his budget by reducing the income-tax exemption
from .£2OO all round to .£125 on incomes under
£425, with no exemption whatever on incomes
above that limit. The rate of the tax is to remain
as heretofore : 4d. in the £ on incomes under £lOOO
derived from personal exertion, and fid. in the £
on any amount above £lOOO ; 9d. in the £ up to
£lOOO on incomes derived from property, and Is.
in the £ on anything above £lOOO. S.A. and
Tasmania are the only provinces which recognise
the just principle that incomes arising from per-
sonal exertion, which are temporary and pre-
carious, should be taxed on a lower scale than
those arising from property, which are more or
less permanent. The owner of the most stupen-
dons intellect that ever existed may fall any day
over a piece of orange-peel and become thereafter
a brain-concussed semi-idiot without the power to
earn a copper, or a man capable of earning £lO,OOO
a year may be destroyed in a second by a 40s.
horse running away with a twopenny omnibus.
The fact to be supremely skylined just
now is that Attoruey-Generalism which dovetails
the administration of justice with party politics
is an institution to be execrated as a damnable
menace to public liberty. Cannot the politicians
read the big capitals of public jeering ?

MY PHILOSOPHY
[Foe The Bulletin.]
If it is true that you’re a flirt
And sweetly uioek my wooiflg,
I vow I’m not the least hit hurt
At what they say you’re doing.
It may be so—your pretty speech
May be deceitful patter,
May be the same to all and each ;
But if it is, what matter?
I’d still woo on, though this were true,
And every doubt I’d smother :
For rather I’d be fooled by you
Than loved by any other.
Vic.
WYVIS.


Official Examination
OF
The Mutual Life Insurance
Co. of New York.
(AT THE REQUEST OF THE COMPANY.)
State of New York? Insurance Department.
JAMES F. PIERCE, Superintendent.
Albany, N.Y., July 17, 1895.
For about eighteen months the available force
of the department has been engaged upon a
minute examination of The Mutual Life Insur-
ance Company of New York. This examination
was begun and conducted substantially to a con-
clusion by the late Michael Shannon, my deputy,
who for many years had been the Chief Examiner
of the department. His sudden death just at the
conclusion of this investigation, and before the
facts and figures which he had collected had been
finally formulated by him, throws upon the
Superintendent the duty of presenting the results
in an intelligible form. The thoroughness and
accuracy of Mr. Shannon’s methods make it neces-
sary to do hardly more than to arrange his own
conclusions.
The examination relates to the condition of the
Company at the beginning of this calendar year ;
the date is coincident with that of the Company’s
annual statement for 1895. The results of the
official examination substantially coincide with
the Company’s statement. The custom of the
department, in such examinations, is not to
depend upon the book entries, but directly to
scrutinise the property, real and personal, which
the books represent as assets, to determine the
liabilities according to the standard of the de-
partment, and to examine and investigate all the
disbursements of the Company.
The traditional usage of the department, though
not prescribed by law, excludes from a Company’s
assets all items whose cash value cannot be tested
by the examination ; Hence the entire amount of
commuted commissions, of agents’ balances and
the loading of premiums, deferred or in collection,
have been deducted from the Company’s Assets
in the tables of the department.
Real Estate.
The Company’s holdings and real property con-
sist (1) of lands and buildings purchased for its
own use and buildings erected for its own offices,
and (2) lands and buildings purchased under fore-
closure of loans.
Office Buildings.
The first class includes the principal building on
Nassau, Cedar and Liberty Streets, and the former
principal office on Broadway and Liberty Street,
both in the City of New York ; a building in
Boston, one in Philadelphia, one in San Fran-
cisco, and three of moderate cost in foreign
countries—viz., one in Berlin, one in the city of
Mexico, and the third in Sydney, Australia. I
have carefully investigated the value of each of
these properties with the aid of the best experts,
and believe them all to have the value assigned to
them on the Company’s books. I find no evidence
of unwise expenditure upon either of these pro-
perties.
In 1880 the Company purchased from the United
States the old Post Office building, and erected
upon its site the Home Office, about one hundred
and ninety-seven feet front by one hundred and
fifteen feet deep. The neighbourhood was then
beyond the financial centre of the city, but this
building gave it a new character, attracting many
corporations, banking firms and professional men.
The Company has since made purchases of addi-
tional land adjoining its offices and large additions
to its building. The land in the vicinity has
steadily increased in value. The appraiser of the
department has valued the entire property now
held by the Company as its principal building at
a sum higher than that at which it is carried on
the books. The building on Broadway which was
formerly the Company’s Home Office, is also
valued by us at more than the Company gives
itself credit for.
Foreclosed Property.
The Company’s method of dealing with this
class of property illustrates its wise conservatism,
and has my approval.
The cash value of this real estate as determined
by the appraisers for the State is in the aggregate
more than it represents on the Company’s boobs
and statements. While the department in this
report cannot recognise prospective increase of
value as an element in its appraisals, it is yet the
opinion of the Superintendent, founded on the
detailed returns of the experts whom he has em-
ployed, that the valuation is one which in the
aggregate no probable event in the future will
reduce, while circumstances are likely to arise
which will materially increase it.
Stocks and Moneyed Corporations.
In recent years the Company has made invest-
ments in the shares of certain financial institu-
tions, These are among the best-known of the
strong financial institutions of New York, and the
shares of each yield in dividends or additions to
surplus a fair percentage on their cost. After ex-
amination I find that each of these purchases of
stock has proved a good investment. In no case
has the Company credited itself upon its books or
in its statements with the full market value of
such stocks. Its estimate in each case is conser-
vative, and is less by a considerable margin than
the price of the stock in the open market.
The same disposition to conservatism has pre-
vailed in the Company’s valuation of all its stocks
and bonds. It is an evidence of judicious fore-
sight in the Company to carry all its stocks and
bonds upon valuations which are not likely to
prove too high even in case of a panic. I approve
its policy in this respect. No item included in the
bonds and stocks owned is over-valued ; in fact,
all of them are appraised by the Company at a
lower rate than current sales would justify.
Cask on Hand and on Deposit.
The items of cash on band and on deposit and
interest in banks and trust companies have been
carefully examined, and the entries in the books
of the Company verified by a careful count of cash,
and by an examination of the books of the deposi-
taries. The department has also inquired into the
standing and security of the banks and trust com-
panies selected as depositaries, and into the rateof
interest obtained and finds tiiat the perfect safety
of the funds has been the first consideration of the
Company, and that it has avoided taking a risk
for the sake of higher rates of interest upon the
large sums awaiting investment.
Loans on Real Estate Security.
The examiners have verified every item on the
Company’s books claiming a lien on real estate and
find that the loans thus secured and drawing in-
terest amount to $71,339,415.92 in exact accord-
ance with the Company’s Statement. A very large
amount of time and labor has been employed by
the department in examining the titles and ap-
praising the values of the five thousand nine
hundred and seventy pieces of property upon
which these loans have been made. In this work
the department has employed the highest skill it
could command and the results are satisfactory.
Safcguards.
The department has investigated the methods
employed by the Company in protecting its col-
lections, balances and remittances and in con-
trolling its agency accounts and expenditure, and
cordially approves the system, under which there
has been substantial exemption from loss ever
since it was organised.
Medical Examinations.
The entire subject of the conduct of the medical
examinations of candidates for insurance has been
investigated by a special examiner of exceptional
competence, Dr. E. B. Rankin.
Business in Foreign Countries.
While The Mutual Life Insurance Company of
New York has for many years offered the privi-
lege of membership to healthy lives in all parts of
the civilised world, it is within the limits of the
United States that it is best known and that its
activity is greatest.
The department has, nevertheless, deemed it
essential to inquire minutely into the character of
the foreign business. As the result of such in-
quiry, I find that the Company in all its foreign
business has practised the same sound and conser-
vative methods which have always characterised
its business in the United States.
Income, Disbursements, Etc.
There is nothing in the items of the Company’s
Statement to require special comment here, inas-
much as the only result of the examination has
been to verify and confirm the figures of each item
as computed or estimated by the Company itself.
Without enlarging, therefore, upon the several
entries, I reier to the tables of Assets and
Liabilities, of Income and Expenditure, wlncb are
given below, remarking that a thorough examina-
tion of the expense and disbursement accounts
shows no instance of any unlawful, illegitimate or
improper expenditure, and that each several entry
has been subjected to rigid and thorough scrutiny
in all its details.
The prosecution to a final completion of the
work which Mr. Shannon had so thoroughly
planned and so nearly finished has only confirmed
the conclusions arrived at by him. The communi-
cation to the Legislature and to the public of the
results of such an examination as this, when they
fully justify and strengthen the position which
one ot the foremost financial institutions of the
United States has long held in public confidence,
is perhaps tiie most agreeable dmy of the Superin-
tendent. I take great satisfaction in certifying,
after a minute and laborious examination of The
Mutual Life Insurance Company of New York,
that the Company is solvent and strong and is con-
ducted in all respects in accordance with the law
and sound business principles. In each instance
in which no difference has been expressly pointed
out in this Report between the conclusions of the
official examination and the statements already
published by the Company, each of those state-
ments has been by that examination completely
tested and proved accurate. No officer or em-
ploye of the Company receives any emolument or
perquisite other than the salary awarded to him
by the Board of Trustees, and no Trustee or
Officer of the Company is permitted to receive or
does receive any commission on loans, insurance
or any other of its transactions.
Every book, paper, document and record in the
Company’s possession has been freely opened to
the examiners of the department, and the Execu-
tive Officers and Official Staff have given cheer-
ful assistance when required. The books are kept
with system, accuracy and simplicity, and show
the condition of each account precisely as it exists,
without resort to bookkeeping devices to vary or
modify the intent and effect of any debit or credit
entry. t
I he Company has prosecuted its affairs upon a
high plane of propriety, avoiding doubtful practices
and unseemly methods, always seeking to uphold
its own dignity as a beneficent and economic factor
in developing the civilisation of out age and
country. Every branch of its nusim-ss is admin-
istered with ability in the interest of its members,
and a wise and vigorous executive management
controls all its departments and combines their
energies in administering its great trust. It
merits in all respects the confidence of the insured
and of the community at large. And, in con-
clusion, I, James F. Pierce, Superintendent of
the Insurance Department of the State of New
York, do hereby certify that the true condition of
The Mutual Life Insurance Company of New
York, after the examination hereinbefore recited,
was upon the 31st day of December, A.D., 1894,
according to the appraisal of its Assets made by
this department, and the valuations of its
Liabilities, also made by said Department, pursu-
ant to the standard established by the laws of the
State of New York, as follows :

Principal Agency in Australia:
Company’s Bailding, Martin Place & Pitt-st.,
SYDNEY.
Z. C. RENNIE, General Manager.
A Great Typewriter
Company.
The United Typewriter and Supplies Com-
pany of New York, the premier firm of its kind in
America and Europe-with branches in the chief
cities of the world is now firmly in
Australia, where the principal office is at 114 Pitt-
street, Sydney, opposite G.P.O. It claims that its
EXTERIOR VIEW.
r.hreetnachines, theCaligraph, Yost, and Densmore,
are each the best of its type. The company,
which, by the way, has taken over Mr. J. E. Cun-
n high am’s Caligraph Agency, sells any one
machine without prejudice; gives the intending
. . . * , . •, ,i c • !
purchaser impartial advice, suit* the pro essiona
or business man with the type of machine whose
special attributes are adapted to his particular
necessities Once Henry George’s wants were
known, he was fitted with a Yost so satisfactorily
’., . .
that he clung to it when the coach lie was on
overturned ; he fell not upon his head, but upon
the Yost. He pronounces it invaluable.
# * *
Per minute the average penman dis-
figures paper witli 15 to 20 scrawly words; the
, it , . ■ . an
average, half-awake ypist scores 40 to 60 pertect
imjiagc, ii<ui . 1
words. For one minute the real expert penman
gqos 50 words, which no one, not even himself,
can read afterwards; whereas the expert typist
clicks off 180 words—clear, sharp, beautiful. More-
i
over, the typist using carbon paper simultaneously
strikes off 20 of his artistic copies, while the
expert pen-criminal sweats blood and ink over his
spider-legged one. Further, the typist using
Edison’s Mimeograph records 3000 perfect copies.
So, compared with the typewriter, the pen is
little more than a burnt stick. The pen' is
very well in its humble way, being, indeed,
mightier than the sword, but the typewriter is
incomparably mightier than the pen, and the
lovely girl who works it—but we’ll let that pass.
# # #
Typewriting takes less space than pen
scrawling, its paper is cheaper, so firms with cor-
respondence daily above one sheet save on
stationery and postage; also typewriting press-
copies much more neatly than pen apologies. The
typewriter changes mail-day, the merchant’s curse,
into holiday, for the mail can be dictated 70 words
per minute.
• * #
To support the typewriter the minds of
the professional and commercial world come forth
in evidence. The author points to his 1500 lines
poem—the great little thing of his own —the
scientist to his treatise, the barrister to bis brief,
the solicitor to his abstract, the banker to his
balance - sheet, the Government office to the
inspector’s report, the limited company to the
statement, the commercial man to the inventory
and invoice, the parson to his sermon, the young
man to the love-letter, the recording-angel to the
sins of the world.
* * *
Why is ifc not universally used ? Because,
up to a few years ago, most members of civilised
nations could sign their names only with a cross.
Typewriting is a revolution, which has come so
suddenly on pen-writing that the human race,
overjoyed at being able to scrawl out its emoti' ns
all by itself, won’t giveup that triumph yetawhiie.
In fact, the typewriter has to be sprung upon the
average human being with considerable caution ;
one island missionary, on first seeing it, flung over
his adonted belief in Christianity, knelt down and
worshipped “The Iron God who writes,” By
and bye, however, the typewriter, like all other
valuable innovations, will safely recommend itself
everywhere, and then it will become as popular as
the pen in its day.
# * #
The typewriter is in this age of effective
machines singularly effective. It saves time, life,
souls, money, breach of promise cases. It is good
business —the clergy have said it. One leading
Australian clergyman says “the Caligraph has
more points of utility and greater durability than
any other typewriter I have ever seen.” Another
says, “ it delivered me from my terrible foe, the
writer’s cramp ; it shortened my work one half,
and I always forgot its cost in the pleasure I found
in its constant use.” Scotch gentleman, too, this
one! And no one is prouder of his Caligraph
than the Sultan of Morocco, who clicks off daily
manifold copies of the orders to the harem.
• • •
It saves time, compared with the pen—.4o
minutes per hour, 1.8 hours per day, 243 days per
year, 46 years out of 70, the compound interest
and capital of the bad language and crying caused
by the average man’s bad writing, and the long
account to be settled thereon in the great here-
after. No business man with an eye to money or
dispatch raisisa word against the typewriter. The
only valid objection was from a countryman who
wouldn’t have it because it wouldn’t spell.
• # #
The new Yost No. 4, so signally success-
ful in England, has some striking testi-
menials. Rider Haggard says: “The Yost
has proved most satisfactory.” B. L. Far-
jeon : “The Yost typewriter is admirable.”
Mrs. Campbell - Praed ; “ The Yost has
delighted me.” Sir William (great gun) Arm-
strong: “It is the royal road in writing.” The
Rev. Curnock. editor Methodist Recorder ; “ It
is clear, strong, quickly learned, easily handled ;
a young girl learned to use it in one day, and in a
few days acquired fair speed.” The President of
St. John’s College, Oxford, says: “I carry it
about with me wherever I stay, and had nodiffi-
eulty in learning the use of it.” Mr. Robert
Gladstone, nephew of the Grand Old Man,
8.C.L., M. A., Oxford, certifies: “It is the per-
fection of simplicity and ingenuity.” It was
with a Yost that Pinero wrote “ The Second Mrs.
Tanqueray.” The Yost has been the
English, French, Indian, and Colonial Govern-
ments, the principal railway and engineering com-
panics, over 20,000 professional and business men,
many earls, and the Emperor of Germany.
Though only five years old, it has gained seven
gold medals.

Altogether the typewriter is one of the
most useful achievements of science, and the
United Typewriter and Supplies Co., pushing fer-
ward the best machines on the almost new field of

OUfc most of his plans anil schemes in railway*

bed. For hours after turning in be is unable to

That charming old lady, Mrs. Louisa Ann
Meredith, poetess, flower-painter, and authoress
of “ The Flora of Tasmania,” died the other day
in Melbourne, aged 83. She came to_N.S.W.
with her husband, Charles Meredith, in 1839,
and to Tasmania in 1840. He died in 1879, and
since that time Mrs. Meredith has been in receipt
of .£lOO pension from Tas. Govt, in recognition of
her literary work. She was the first and only lady
elected as a member of the Tas. Royal Society.

The balance sheet on 30tli September, and profit and loss account for the half-year which then
terminated are now submitted to the shareholders.

The calls on the ordinary shares continue to be paid' with regularity ; the sum received during the
half-year amounted to .£43,936 ss. 5d., and the entire paid-up capital of the bank now stands at
.£1 821 721 Os 9d
’'During the half-year a branch at Ores wick, with agency at Allendale, has been opened in Victoria,
and the branches at Balaklava, Border Town, Carrilton, Laura, Mmlaton, Wallaroo and Willunga,
in South Australia, have been closed. . ~ ~ ... , .
As compared with the twelve months just prior to reconstruction, the expenditure shows contrac-
tion at the rate of .£36,000 a year, and 40 branches have been closed since the period referred to.
There are indications of improvement in trade and in the prospects of the producing interests,
which may be marred if the exceptionally dry season now prevailing continues. The value of real
property however cannot yet be said to have been affected favorably, and the directors, therefore,
think it advisable to withdraw the reserve fund of .£lOO,OOO, and to add that amount to the sum held
in office reserves towards the equalisation of the dependencies of the bank. ,
The Ordinary General Meeting was held on Wednesday, Gth November, at noon, at the head
office, Collins-street, Melbourne. . , ~
By order of the Board of Directors. F. G. SMITH, Chief Manager.
The National Bank of Australasia Limited. Head Office, Melbourne, October 24, 1895.

We certify that vve have examined the accounts of the National Bank of Australasia Limited for
the half-year ending 30th September, 1895, together with the statement of profit and loss and the
aggregate balance sheet for the same period (the assets and liabilities at the branches are taken on the
certificates of the managers and accountants), and that in our opinion the balance sheet is a full and
fair balance sheet, containing the particulars required by the Articles of Association, and properly
drawn up so as to exhibit a true and correct view of the state of the bank’s affairs.
W. H. TUOKETT,
ANDREW BURNS.

train has made the distance between New York
and Buffalo, 436 J miles, in 407 minutes, or at an
average speed of 64 1-3 miles per hour. English
record, 63 1-3 miles per hour.
“Jimmy” Taylor, M.L.O. (Q.), who died
recently, was known as king of Toowoomba,
as, up to a few years ago, he practically owned
the red-soil township. For the last three years
he was wheeled about in a bath-chair, and died by
inches. He was “ humping bluey ” when Kings-
ley wrote “Geoffrey fiamlyn.” Jimmy stuck
fondly to two old-time favorites—a battered felt
wideawake and a short-stemmed soot-black clay.
Often he horrified his latter-day aristocratic rela-
tives by turning up at social functions in a dingy
suit of tweed and feeling vainly in his pockets for
a light. He rather astonished vice-royalty once
by saying, “ How do, Sir ——, got a match ? ”
For coughs, colds you can’t beat Colemane
and Sons Eucalypte Extract.*
Says an S.A. paper :
Since we have enjoyed the luxury of paid members of
Parliament our National Debt has enormously increased,
wages and salaries have been lowered, our sheep have
diminished in value, our exporting producers have been
taxed, hampered, and worried by taxation and every
other possible way; land-values have declined; and
money has been withdrawn. Were it not that from out-
side causes interest of money has also declined, and
Broken Hill, poor South Australia would by this time
have been like some unfortunate water-logged derelict.
It is yet time by prompt and resolute action to save her
from insolvency.
All these things—even the fall in the values of
sheep and land because wool and meat and wheat
have gone down in London—have taken place
since S. A. had payment of members. Therefore,
they are caused by payment of members. Also,
they have all taken place since Queen Victoria
came to the British throne. Therefore, they are
equally caused by Victoria being on the throne.
Consequently, let Victoria be abolished and the
price of wool will go up. Logic is a great institu-
tion.
The only known cure for influenza is Cole-
mane and Sons Eucalypte Extract.
Bishop Julius, of Christchurch (M.L.), on
the plate;—
I never saw so much bad coin in all my life. To offer
to the Church—the cause of God—money that the baker
or the butcher would not accept shocks me. The man
who would do that sort of thing wants the grace of God—
or three months.
There is a fund of unconscious humor about a
bishop who thinks that the grace of God or three
months’ hard labor will answer equally well.
Probably they would answer equally well, but the
bottom would fall out of the bishop industry if
the public got to know of it.

NOW, WHO IS CHAMPION?
Keefs at Sheep-station Creek, anew Vic. field,
are turning out some payable battery stone. Of three
recent erus’lings, a claim on Milne’s line turned in 950 z.
from 42 tons, while the Highland Chief 91ft, body of
stone lias averaged 6oz. for small orusbings ; and the
Golden King has topped with a fine yield of 296 si.
from 12$ tons. Strange to say, the Golden King has been
abandoned twice, and ft couple of feet driving in the old
workings brought the new party on to the present rich
shoot. One never knows what the next pick-stroke will
reveal. Sheep-station gold is very fine and worth about
£4 ss. an oz , which heats the Beechworth gold, always
ranked the dearest by the Mint.

• * • •
Maoriland proposes giving a practical start to
tlie heet-sugar industry by offering a prize of £50,000 for
the first 10,000 tons of locally-produced and refined sugar.
The beet industry is worth cultivating; Australasia
spends £1,340,000 a year on foreign nigger-grown sugar,
and all this money might be kept in lhe country, and
work and good wages thereby afforded to some 13,000
people who are very much in want of both.

A Chinese miner in M’land advertises a crush-
ing plant for sale in the Australian Chinese paper, KWONG
Yick Wah Bo: “I have a gold making instrument for
sale for £2OO. It has five falling hammers which smash
the stone and pick out the gold with the aid of quick-
silver and the gold together, and if there is one hundred
pounds worth of gold in the stone it will give you £9B
worth. It is a lucky machine, and all who have owned it
have prosperously mined and have had many dutiful
children.”

Visitors to the Spouting Spring at Heli-
don, 70 miles from Brisbane, are always astounded
at the quantity of water they can consume at the
Spring. Ten or 12 tumblers in an hour or so are
taken with comfort. i'he reason is that the
tissues of the body are absorbing the water all the
time. Something beneficial is always added to
the system by every glass of Helidou Spa water
consumed. Messrs, Tooth and Co., Kent
Brewery, are the Sydney agents.

An examination into the business opera-
tions and present position of the Mutual Life In-
surance Company of New York has just been
instituted by the Insurance Department of the
State of New York, at the request of the com-
pany The investigation was of the most careful
character, engaging the attention of the Depart-
ment for about eighteen months, and the result
was to verify, in every particular, the statement
issued by the company’s executive at the close of
last year. In the report, which is dated July 17th,
1895, the Superintendent of the State Insurance
Department, Hon. James F. Fierce, rema' ks
“'l’he communication to the Legislature and to
the public of the results of such « n examination as
this, when they fully justify and strengthen the
position which one of the foremost financial insti-
tutions of the United States has long held in pub-
lic confidence, is perhaps the most ag ecable duty
of the Superintendent.” The report, ivhich is in-
teresting reading, will be found set forth in full
on page 17 of this issue of The Bulletin.®
IMPORTANCE OF DIPPING SHEEP.
WRITING upon the above subject, the National
Wool Company warns farmers about the impor-
tance of keeping their sheep free from ticks and
other parasites by dipping their flocks regularly
after shearing. It is estimated that an animal
kept healthy by this useful economy will give a
return of at least Is. fid. per head more than one
suffering from these parasities; a clean sheep keeps
in better condition, produces a heavier fleece and
one which will bring higher value than a diseased
animal’s. It is pointed out that wool-buyers are
more exacting in their selection of wools than
heretofore, and insist upon careful preparation.
The lesson of the day taught by depression and
low prices is that sheep farmers and agriculturists
must regulate their operations on a scientific basis,
must turn out their products in fir>t-class style,
and must exercise frugality and care in every step,
both of production and distribution. Sheep farmers
require suitable breeds of sheep for the country
they occupy, and should institute a thorough system
of culling, dipping, and avoiding overstocking,
and applying intelligence to the sorting, skirting
and classing of the fleeces. Under these condi-
tions sheep farming will pay handsomely, oven at
the present low’ prices of wool. The National
Wool Company wish to put fanners on the right
track, and recommend “ Odam’s ” dip.*

Music and Quiver supplied the two most in-

and twenty of them in the last half-mile, when its victory
was out of the question.

The old school of English turfmen would he dis-
posed to argue that horse-racing, as conducted by rough-
and-tumble Jockeys on circular Australian courses, isn’t
sportatall. Tnere must always be a “gloiious uncer-
tainty ” about horse contests under the best of citcum-
siances. Every result would be somewhat different, even
though the positions might not he reversed, if a race were
run over again. But racing, as we get it in this country
nowadays, is not so much a trial of merit (tempered by
legit inai e luck chances) b lween the goers, as a Inal of
luck aggravated by iccklessaiid incompetent jockey ship.
Tile first tniag a punier prays for is the safely of his
equiti • “fancy” wn n it shall, come jostling round the
turn. Fads in flat-races are incidents that bookmakers
reckon upon as a malt r of course during a big meeting.
“Is the favorite d wn?” asks jubilant ikey' Mo of bis
clerk, just as though he were belting on a steeple-
chase. Accidents are always happening. Four horses
fell, last Thursday, in the Vet ran Slakes, run over the
Derby course, and the writer, whose investment came to
grief along with The Trier, remembered bow, in days of
yore, he had seen Ids money go down with Grace Darling
in the very snne race. Which set him thinking of the
Melbourne Cup (Qleidoth’s) that Porlsea lost through a
fall—the wiiler hacked Porlsea, by the way—and then his
thoughts naturally reverted to tliis year’s Cup, wherein
Auraria, says jockey Stevenson, was nearly knocked over.
Tins sort of thing isn’t sport. It’s merely wild, farcical
gambling, with an occasional element of tragedy iu it.
Tlie “ books,” who are the best judges of racirg
performances, are unanimous iu declaring that no modern
Austialian jockey is comparable to the Hales, O Brien,
Gorry, or Donald Nicholson of a few years ago. If those
artists, or their ghosts, were to start riding again in their
best form, they would have to he sp-cially penalised.
The notice hoard would have to announce, “Number so-
and-so carries 1410. extra because Hales is up.” Probably
Stevenson, whose photo, was published iu The Bulletin
a few mouths hack, rides with more nerve and judgment
than the oilier hoys, whilst Gough Can give points to tlie
beslavered Jimmy Hayes and ot iers of the heavy weights,
hut the average standard of ability in the saddle is, un-
doubtedly, much lower than formerly.

Very few punters have the necessary pluck to
back their fancy at what is called “a tempting price.”
Auraria was a case in point. The South Australian had
an excellent chance to win the Cnp, but the mere fact
that Ikey Mo could afford to lay 40 to 1 against her
frightened the public away. It is the short price that
tempts an unreasoning investor, not the long one.

winners was very satisfactory as a spectacle. The grand-

struggled past the posl. When the result was announced
the Hill and Flat clapped their hands and w'ere joyful

affirm that, she deserved to win ttie credit of beating Wal-
lace. Auraria was only a girl, a delicate, beautiful female

in the very pdme of life and condition. She had under-
taken (o heat them on their merits. They were ex-
pected to do their level best from the slart, just to
oblige the lady, as she would repeat her Melbourne Cup
performance. But, after going about halt a furlong, the
poor girl discovered that tire three fellows were afraid to
-gallop their hardest, and so she went ahead to prevent

encotingemenl ; Auraria p gged away monotonously in

Oscillator first of a I, then Hova, and Wallace in

he’s h'aten, made a long, desperate effort to catch

The V.R.C. Handicap, last Saturday, showed
the folly of backing horses on the strength ot their
stamina for races which are quite likely to he run in slow
(imp. Waterfall, Burrahari, and Palo Alto carried a lot
of money, and yet, nothing was sent lo ensure a pace for
eitnei of them. The victory of Onward, a speedy non-
stayer w ith a light weight up, was a wholesome pill for
Messrs. Oxenham, Purdies, and Redfearn. Ttiey liacked
their animals for a If-mile race, without taking care to get
the article required, and a six furlongs canter followed by
a mile gallop was the run they had for their money.
Oxenham’s want of foresight is unaccountable. He knows
very well that none of the good racing generals would
dispense with a pace-maker under such circumstances.
They either run one of their own, or make arrangements
to have somebody else’s horse rushed along at top speed.
The races that Hova’s owners wilfully threw
away were the Toorak Handicap and Caulfield Cup.
They were dead-birds for the speedy horse, barring
accidents. Tim Bulletin bad a paragraph re Hova and
the Toorak Handicap in type when news came that the
clever people weren’t going to run their cert.

his absence from the V.R.C. meeting this year.

would have another go at Wallace in either of the next
weigbt-for-age events the Derby winner was stripped for.
The sporting penciller, dissatisfied with the Derby run-
ning and anxious to justify his confident Waterfall “ lip, ’
told W. R. Wilson that he intended to take down Wallace
anyway on the Saturday. But he changed his plans and
sent tlie little, level-backed, Caulfield surprise for the
V.R.C. Handicap, only to get beaten in a falsely-run race.
Oxenham had the worst of luck, as a hacker, throughout
the meeting.

back with such a firm belief in the super-excellence of
that heavy, camel humped animal. Quiver is another
generally over-rated gee-gee. She’s a good one, but not
a smasher.
The voice of Mossy Isaacs, calling the odds
“straight out’’ for Melbourne Cup, sounded quite
strange. O i all other days of the year old Mossy yells
“ Lay the double 1 ”
Here and there punters took “20 to 1 the
double” about either Auraria or Wallace for the C. B.
Fisher Plate, coupled with Parisienne for the next race.
Parish nne won ihe next race, and those punters, getting
half their bets, made £9 10s. profit on the sovereign.
Parisienne started at fifteens, sixteens, and twenties to
one. Laying the double is a comfortable game.
Miller’s neat little “Monthly Racing Chronicle’’
for October was ready by Derby Day tNov. 2), and sub-
scribers went to Flemingtou with the “latest form” in
their pockets.
* • • *
The V.R.C. has now raced itself out of debt.
• * • •
Wallace’s dead-beat with Auraria and defeat of
Hova, on the last day of the V.R.C. meeting, should set
at rest all doubts about his gameness and staying power.
• * * *
Melh. Cup meeting shows the tenacity of bad
luck. J. B. Clark’s hr. g. Reconstruction within one
week ran second in Coburg Stakes, Spring, and Free
Handicaps, besides finishing just behind Music in
Wibiamsiown Cup. After Devon, last year’s second for the
Caulfield, Melb., and Williamstown Cups, and Monastery,
three times “ pipped ” on the post at Adelaide Cup time,
Reconstruction seems the unluckiest horse tacing.

• « * •
The Sydney Daily Telegraph was properly
"had” in its apparent "discovery” of a “two-up”
school on a large scale in the city. In its next issue it
acknowledged that the police had long been aware
of the concern, but owing to a technicality in the bet-
king-law, were unable to obtain a conviction against the
far-seeing proprietors.
The school is an ingeniously-got-np affair which
appeals purely to the larrikin class, and is run by three
people closely related to a big municipal official. The
entrance to the establishment is through a tobacconist’s
shop, where any new customer is examined and ques-
tioned before being further admitted. If the visitor is a
suspected police emissary an electric-bell gives warning
to the room above, before entry is given, when the place
will be found to be occupied as a free-and-easy club. One
individual will be thrumming on an ancient piano, others
will be boxing and using dumb-bells, and some playing
dominoes and kindred games. This little stratagem is
kept up till the M.C. asks the caller if he wants to be pro-
posed, and it he has the usual ss. nomination fee 1 A
speedy exit generally follows.
The sudden anti-gambling spasm of Sydney
D.T. was paradoxically timely. The very issue that re-
ported the running for Melb. Cup, and the glories of
Flemington Lawn, told the past speculators the variations
of the betting odds on each animal in each event, and
even interviewed the Cup-winning jockey! —that very
identical issue came down with two virtuous hoofs on the
proletarian game of “heading ’em,” under big-type
captions.
* * * •
N.S.W. gaming law. You mustn’t run a tote in
a shop, or on the footpath, but you may run a dozen
totes down a side alley. Also, you mustn’t “ head ’em ”
in a street, or a lane, or an alley, or a park, but you may
head ’em till all’s blue in a shop.
♦ * ♦ *
Hopscotch has quickly fallen from the pedestal
on which ids admirers placed him after his dual
victory at A. J.O. Spring meeting. The true reason for
these victories seems to be that he was able to be galloped
at Chipping Norton into good condition, whilst his
opponents at Randwick were being so hammered about
on the hard ground that they were nearly all infirm at
race-time.
• • ♦ •
The V.R.O. running of one or two of the
N.S.W. brigade caused much adverse comment, although
no official notice was taken of their “curious” perform-
ances. It will he interesting to note how they perform at
the A.J.C. Summer and Tattersall’s meeting. “ Come on
over two stone ” (in six weeks) will doubtless be the
excuse.
• • • •
Kan.akaland will not lose sweep postage-profits
if rnv compromise is possible; so, having just suppressed
con ultaliens by Act, Parliament is invited to act some
more and un-suppress them. The new bill provides that
the Stamp Commissioners may license approved pro-
moters, who must give bond for payment of the prizes,
pay all receipts to trust account, submit the drawing-
machinery for approval, and pay £5OO to the revenue if
they distribute less than £50,000 ; £lOOO if more.

The handicapping at Lillie Bridge, Sydney, is
demoralising the weekly trot. To place Yankee on
scratch, and Coolgardie on 40sec. in a mile and a half,
was simply setting Coolgardie an impossible task. Yankee
D reckoned to be able to trot a mile in about 3min. ssec.
or 4min. 37|sec. forthemile-and-a-half. Coolgardie, 40sec.
behind, would have to trot about a 2.38 gait to win,
round a quarter-mile course with sharp turns.
• • • •
David James, owner of the Melb. Cup
winner, Auraria, is reported to bet very moderately,
and to have won little more than the stake by
khe mare’s victory. The man who drew Auraria in
the big sweep offered to lay her owner £3OOO to i 5O, but
ne refused. As a matter of fact, Mr. James fancied the
mare more for the Derby than the Cup. His win was
popular in Adelaide, as he was one of the chief movers in
bue S.A. totalisator revival a jear or two ago.

The “Victorian Athletic League” has been
formed to supervise professional pedestrianism. H keeps
a register of all pros., their real names, residences for
years, records and appearance. Most leading country
clubs have joined for mutual prolection. The gaol-
records should be of service in this matter.
» ♦ * •
The dream again, d la Craig’s Nimblefoot, and
genuine, apparently. A St. Kilda man dreamt the double
Bruin-Auraria, Caulfield and Melb. Cups, and that he
would die, which he did. All this was published before
Caulfield, llis Caulfield lip didn’t come off. Such is the
half-truth way of dreams in the experience of everyone.
* • * *
Paris has been scratched again—this time for
Liverpool Cup. Tim Bulletin begins to suspect that the
galloping-machine is being pursued by the Dean curse.
• * • •
Ikey Mo was cheerfully sarcastic over the Oaks
race. When Auraria came down the straight with a long,
easy lead, Ikey indulged in such phasaniries as “10 to 1
agin’ the favorite 1 ” : “ There’s generally a ‘turn-up’ for
the Oaks” : “ No depending on fillies for this race,” and
so forth.
* • * *
Megson, N.S.W. champion cyclist, is counted
by his supporters the fastest man in Australia from a
quarter-mile to two miles. In condition and on a grass
track they say Megson has only been gruelled once —by
Faisons twelve months ago in the ten-mile race. But,
judged on his performances, Megson cannot last over two
miles at a hot pace, and the way the half-trained Lewis
smothered him the other day in the Goulburn three miles
corroborates previous evidence. Megson’s riding in this
race showed that all form had been trained out of him, so
this week (Nov. 9 16) Megson goes easy so as to come
fresh against Zimmerman on Saturday. What distin-
guishes Megson among cyclists is his gameness rather
than his skill. Megson’s race is a slow-run race with a
red-hot finishing spurt. He gels the chance of his life
against the imperfectly-trained Zim this Saturday.
• • • •
Cyclist Parsons, who has now added to his list
of laurels the 50-mile championship of Victoria (he did
the distance in 2hr. 7min. 47see., easily beating Martin,
the Yankee, and Porloa, the Italian champion), is a
Sydney native, but has spent half his life in Melbourne,
where, three years ago, he saw- the late Harry Lambion
win the Austral for the second time. Hamilton's perform-
ance inspired the youthful Prisons to own a hike, and
when he got one he set to work with a will. In his first,
a novice race. Parsons finished second ; next time he
stepped up one, and from that time onwards he has been
steadily coming to the frent, His win of (he 50-mile
Road Race first hrou ;ht him into prominence ; then his
performance with the Victory Quartette in the Inter-Club
contest, and numerous small wins, pulled him back to ihe
20yd. mark in last year’s Austral. In that race Parsons
performed badly owing lo illness, hut in the 10 miles
championship, later in the same meeting, he created what
was possibly the greatest surprise that ever took place on
a Vic. cycle track. Paced by tandems, which he pressed
closely throughout, he twice lapped Sharp, the previous
year’s winner, and established the existing world’s grass
record. A few' weeks later, Parsons had a cut at all the
Australian records from £ to 10 miles, hell by Wally
Kerr, the Sydney amateur, and managed to lower all
substantially, on the five-lap, undished, broken-surfaced
Exhibition track. To those who know Parsons’ capabili-
ties, Zimmerman’s defeat over the longer distance, at
Adelaide, comes as no surprise, and tbeprevailingopinion
in Melb. is that on the grass track Parsons can hold his
own with the Yankee champion at any distance and at
any time. Parsons is a strongly built and handsome
young fellow, going about 1401 b. in condition, but he is
lamentably lacking in that abnormal estimation of self
essential to the champion athlete.
« * * «
Parsons’ 50 miles in 2 hours 7min. is a fine per-
formance for the antiquated Melb. Exhibition track, but
it is still far off the English record of 1 hour 56min. Aus-
tralia will not get near the world’s records until a decent
cement track, high-banked and with a good straight, is
laid down.
* * • •
What Zimmerman thinks of Parsons is best
gauged from Zim. having persuaded Parsons into riding a
Nimrod machine. Parsons was given his pick— gratis.
* * * •
Zimmerman and his manager intend snapping
all the best Australian cyclists to ride Zim.’s brand of
cycle. Two or three Australians have been offered trips
to America, and, if successful there, then on to Europe.

Mrs. Rath, in light cheese green silk looked remarkably
A grand fair in aid of those two highly
deserving charities, the Thirlmere Home for Con-
sumptives and the Fresh Air League, is to be held
at Sydney Exhibition Building from 28ih Nov. to
7th Dec. A very influential committee is at work,
and a number of important society ladies are
to officiate at the stalls. Further details will be
found in our advertising columns. T,he move-
ment merits all the public support it can get, but
the statemement, “No raffles will be allowed,
looks unlucky. Raffles may not be high-toned, but
they scoop in the dross, and dross is better than
tone.
THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE. Un-
doubtedly the largest buyer of opals ’ n tl,e
rough state is H. Newman, the well-known Mel-
bourne jeweller, who has been connected with
this branch of the gem trade since its inception,
and keeps a large staff of skilled lapidaries busily
employed on these and other Australian gems.
He is also a large buyer of pearls and diamonds,
and gets through an immense quantity of Queens-
land sapphires. The firm has been established
for over 40 years, and has a reputation for straight
dealing which is sufficient guarantee to con-
signors of rough gems that they will be honorably
dealt with. The establishment is still at the old
spot in Elizabeth-street, right opposite the Post
Office, and is well worthy of a visit of inspection.*
A CUP HOWL.
Auraria the winner ! Iky Mo another skinner !
For saith not the Holy Scripture: “Of the
Chosen are the Jews ” ?
And they now will keep Passover, and give thanks
unto Gee-Hova,
While the voice of lamentation fills the pulpit
and the pews.
For the ladies, perfect racing, lovely dresses and
tight lacing;
For “The Chosen,” all the shekels, both of
silver and of gold.
For the owners, disappointment—and a blow-fly
in the ointment
For the trainers and the backers, who by time-
tests have been sold.
Lo, the two Cups and the Steeple, have they not
enriched “My People”?
Clad in sackcloth and in ashes now the Gentiles
sadly mourn ;
Yet ’tis but the quaint old story, ever fresh and
ever hoary—
That he who goes to shear a Jew returneth
closely shorn.

It is announced that the veteran bride-
groom Parkes’s sources of income at present com- |
prise the interest on what was originally £14,000,
viz., £lO,OOO subscribed and presented to him some
years ago by citizens of Sydney, and £4OOO, the
amount realised by the sale of his book, “ Fifty
Years in the Making of Australian History.”
Both these sums were invested, but the old man s
children by his second wife only participate in the
interest on the smaller sum. During the bank
crisis of 1893 this fortune was much depreciated,
so that now he only realises interest on some-
thing like ££ooo.
A GRAND LITERARY INSTITUTION.
Cole’s Book Arcade now runs from the centre
of the block in Bourke-street to the centre of the
block in Collins-street, Melb., a distance of 660 ft.,
and contains the following 17 departments, mostly
literary and artistic : 1, New Books; 2, Second-
hand Books; 3, Stationery; 4, Music; 5, Fancy
Goods ; 6, Toys ; 7, Perfumery ; 8, Pictures ; 9,
Artists’ Materials ; 10, an Ornament Exhibition ;
11, a Japanese Exhibition ; 12, a Wonderland ;
13, a Smiling Gallery; 14, a Child’s Picture
Gallery ; 15, a Bargain Room ; 16, a Tea Room ;
17, a splendidly arranged new VVholesale Book
Department, containing half a million books. Ihe
main walks of the establishment are half a mile
long. *
Dr. Harris, to whom Sydney University last
week gave a farewell conversazione, has been over
six years head-master of the King's School, Parra-
'matta. Dr. Harris is probably the most uncom-
promising champion for the study of Latin and
Greek to be found in Australia, maintaining, as
he does, that a good English style is almost im-
possible without the influence of these languages
as acquired by direct study. He came to Aus-
tralia at the invitation of Bishop Barry, and
retires because he thinks himself too old for the
work of the school. King’s School is a historical
institution, dating back over 60 years, and claim-
ing a large share in the moulding of the characters
of many prominent Australians.

“ Woman’s true policy is to lie low, and
let tile male imagination endow her with depths.’’
The passion for sweet scents is one which
has lasted continuously from the days of the
oldest records until now, and the demand to-day
is probably more universal than it ever was before.
The history of perfumery in modern times is prac-
tically the history of the great French house of
Pinaud and Co. It was founded in 1840, and has
grown and increased in prosperity for 55 years,
and now its perfumes are recognised as the best in
the world and its name is known in all civilised
regions to the uttermost ends of the earth.
Among the most famous of its sweet odors are
Violette de haruie, Borghetto, Prisa de las
Pampas, Ixora, and Breoni. Most of the new
inventions in perfumery come from Pinaud’s es-
tablishment —the largest and most complete in
existence —and its label has come to be recognised
as a guarantee of absolute perfection. France is
the centre of the perfumery trade, no other
country, since Italy lost its hold on the business
long ago, having even attempted to seiionsly com-
pete ; and Pinand's is the French perfumery trade
to all intents and purpes s.*

Case. 20 pages, full BuLLKTiN-size. Price, 6d.
Mr. Nathan Herman, the proprietor of the
Misfit Parlor in Wynyard-square, Sydney, will be
remembered in that city for many years as the
man who struck a new idea. In every country
where man is sufficiently civilised to have his
clothes and boots made to order, there is neces-
sarily a large accumulation of garments which
have been made to tit the frame of particular pur-
chasers, who didn’t call for them, or couldn’t pay
for them, or died, or absconded, or went
mad, or got into gaol before delivery, and
so left them on the maker’s hands. And
in every case there is someone else about
whom the rejected article would fit, for
everybody’s clothes—except those of Dick Dead-
eye and the Hunchback of Notre Dame —fit
somebody else, and this person would bo glad to
get a first-class garment at a reduced price, and
the builder of it would be pleased to get rid of it
at almost any sacrifice. The difficulty always has
been to bring the misfit and the man it will fit
together, and Mr. Herman solved the problem by
accumulating in one place misfits from ail
the ends of the earth, so that everybody who
can possibly be fitted by human ingenuity
may go there and be satisfied. He is
also a universal provider—a dealer in safes,
crockery, furniture, bedding, carpets, linoleums,
oil-cloths, drapery, jewellery, watches, cigars,
tobacco, tea (401b. chest for 30s. or 121b. tin for
10s.), cutlery, fancy goods, ironmongery, &c., &c.
But the misfit idea was his own special inspira-
tion, and he is proud of it. Even the famous
£2 bale, which contains a suit of clothes, two
shirts, two undershirts, six pairs socks, two ties,
two collars, six handkerchiefs, set studs, hat, pair
braces and pair of boots, all sent to the railway
station or wharf nearest to the buyer’s residence
for 40s., doesn’t appeal to his soul like that great
original idea. Mr. Herman has lately broken out
into pianos, firearms, ammunition, and other new
departments, and makes gentlemen’s suits for
£1 9s. 6d., and supplies dress-suits at one-fourth
the usual price. Also, lie has broken out into so
many other lines of business of late that no one
knows for certain what he has got, and the things
that he iiasn’t got could mostly be enumerated on
the back of one postage stamp, and even then the
stamp wouldn’t be unduly crowded.*

REVIEW.
Webster’s International
Dictionary .*
(“HOP," OF SYDNEY BULLETIN).
By L. HOPKINS. ESQ.
Sydney, October 31, 1895.
1 am the happy possessor of Webster’s Inter-
national Dictionary, and I need not say I am
delighted with it, so much so that I am reading it
right through. The style is somewhat jerky, but
it is word painting of the highest order. It is
hardly what you might call light literature. I
stopped at the grocer’s on my way home, and got
him to weigh it, and we found that it weighed
rather less than the present Premier of N.S. W.
There are one or two statements in the book
which I cannot allow to go unchallenged ; for
instance, on page 2011 I find the following passage
in Egyptian hieratic. Mr. Webster proceeds to
tell us;—
Nqw my recollection of the circumstance (for I
was pros nt at the time) is—
(The italics are mine). Perhaps Mr. Webster, in
his next edition, will set this very trifling discre-
pancy right. It is the only fault (if fault it be)
which I have found so far. Mr. Webster has left
nothing to the imagination of his readers, for the
narrative is enriched with thousands of illustra-
tions done in the author’s best style, and the
absorbed reader will find a picture of everything,
from a plesiosaurus to a new woman.
Mr. Webster lias produced one of the great
books of the age ; one which is worthy to go down
to posterity with “Robinson Crusoe” and
“ Trilby.”
The author spells out each word carefully, and
explains its meaning as he goes on, and it would
be well if some of our jin-de-siede poets would
follow his example. The book should be in every
schoolboy’s pocket, in every gentleman’s library,
in every counting-house, and wherever letters are
written and words spoken.
•“Webster’s International Dictionary
of the English Language,” being the authentic
edition of “ Webster’s Unabridged Dictionary,”
comprising the issues of 1804, 1879, and 1884;
now thoroughly revised and enlarged under the
supervision of Noah Porter, D.D., LL.D., of
Yale University, with a voluminous Appendix.
Published by (4. and C. Merriam Co., Spring-
field, Mass.; 40 Hunter-street, Sydney.
The Best Xmas Present.
Christmas bells will ring in family reunion.
Young and old look forward with great joy and it
is the keenest endeavour of everyone to pass this
fete in the family circle. When gathered around
the Xmas tree any absent member will be pain-
fully missed, and a sigh given for tho-e who are
away. Nothing, however, interferes with the joy
of Xmas more than if one of the family is stricken
down witli disease, and recovery to health for
such member would no doubt be most appreciated
of all Xmas presents for the whole family.
This valuable present will be found in the
timely employment of Warner’s Safe Cure, where-
by sorrow would be changed into joy. Nearly all
diseases of the human body depend upon dis-
orders of the kidneys, liver, and digestive organs,
being the laboratory for the whole organism.
Warner’s Safe Cure is a sure and reliable remedy
for the cure of these disorders, and ensures good
health.
Follow the example of Mrs, S. G. Cox, of
Pepper Grove, Bacchus Marsh, Victoria. She
says : “I was taken suddenly ill and called in
our doctor, who attended me three months with-
out doing me the least good. He then informed
me that 1 was suffering from chronic disease of
the kidneys, and that having administered every
kind ol medicine likely to be of any service, with-
out any improvement, he considered it would be
useless to continue his attendance. At that time
I was completely wasted away, and in such a
helpless state that I could not leave my bed. I
then commenced, through the advice of friends,
taking Warner’s Safe Cure. The effects which*
followed were marvellous. I can now look back
and say : ‘lf it had not been for this medicine I
should have been in my grave.’ To those suffer-
ing from kidney disease, I earnestly advise them
to give Warner’s Safe Cure a trial before trying
anything else.”*

[FOR THE BULLETIN]
A BUSH “DOCTOR’S” VIGIL.
Yes, thanks; when? Well then, “up to the
pretty ”;
I feel shaky. Just cut me a fill,
Took it well? Yes, she’s plucky, is Kitty.
Pali ! I smell that damned chloroform still I
Oh, / know ! “ There’s no reason to worry,”
I’ve “had many worse cases before.”
But you don’t understand, man—no hurry ;
Don’t go yet. Sit down !—open that do r !
Phew 1 It’s cursedly hot.
She’s just twenty.
She met Fergus at “ Bilby ” last year.
She’d no money, and Fergus had plenty;
They were married in August, down here.
Did you notice her pulse, how it fluttered ?
I examined her heart long ago ;
I remember the threat that it uttered
As that faulty valve clicked to and fro.
iMo, I haven’t told Fergus ; why should I ?
It could do her no possible good.
And I didn’t tell Kitty. How could I?
I’d have rather been shot where I stood !
Yes, of course ! Lots of cases pull through it
After worse times than she had last night ;
But it’s hard lines that I had to do it.
I can see her hand now, gripped so tight
On the curtain

-Amy good in aortic regurge ?
Do you think we might give amyl nitrate?
There ! Don’t laugh, man! My brain’s in a
surge!
That last temperature—eh ? Ninety-seven?
What was that ? I can’t catch what she said

She’s dead, Mack! My Kitty is dead !

IN MULGA TOWN—A SONG.
We played at love in Mulga Town,
And oh ! her eyes were blue,
We played at love in Mulga Town,
And love’s a game for two.
If three should play, alack-a-day !
There’s one of them will rue,
Dear Heart,
There’s one of them will rue.
Three played at love in Mulga Town,
True love they could not hide;
Three played at love in Mulga Town,
Two laughed : the other sighed ;
Though two may woo the wide world through,
But one may kiss the bride,
Dear Heart,
But one may kiss the bride.
Three played at love in Mulga Town,
And one’s too sad to weep ;
Three played at love in Mulga Town—
The creek runs dark and deep ;
So warm she flows no mortal knows
How cold her dead may sleep,
Dear Heart,
How cold her dead may sleep.
N.S.W.
GLENROWAN.
ILLEGITIMATE.
“ The children of those who do not love in marriage
seem to bear an hereditary coldness.” —Thackeray.
We have a right to our father’s name,
But our lives are curst with an inward shame;
He brought to his priest-blessed union lust ;
His bride, our mother, a cold disgust.
God took no heed of a priest’s decree,
But branded our souls with bastardy.
Oar limbs are strong, and our brains are clear,
But our hearts are cold to aught but fear.
Our hearts are cold as the winter mist,
As the hearts of those ttiat loathed and kissed.
Pity us, pity, O Pate unmoved !
The children of those that never loved.
Maoriland.
O. CLYDE.
A WITHERED MUSK-ROSE.
Poor withered rose, how well I can remember
The day when first we met;
It’s full twelve years since last I opened
Horace
Twelve years, and you here yet !
Lord, how it all comes back : the lawn, the lilacs,
All nodding in the breeze,
And I, beneath their welcome shade reclining,
Head Horace at my ease.
How fair she looked, dressed how I don’t
remember—
I think it was in white—
And how she plucked you and you pricked her
finger;
Egad ! you did quite right,
’Twas rather rough to stamp upon you lying
A-withering in the dew ;
Poor little rose, and so she crushed you, did she?
Ah, well—she crushed me, too.
At least, I thought so then— prudens futuri —
It doesn’t matter now.
When we meet nowadays—ah me, the pity !
I dare not even bow.
Eheu, eheu J —to think I’m ten years married !
I’ve seen her—in the dusk,
The air around her tainted with the fragrance
Of patchouli and musk.
A musk-rose—well, poor prophet, thus I burn you,
So, God forgive us all
And keep us (if we let Him) from temptation ;
Even a star can fall!
Cue, Westralia.

Scared!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Mutfcon-birdmg was in full swing in Bass’
Straits. One morning, Davis, to whom the busi-
ness was novel, accompanied a party of half-castes
to one of the many small islands on which the
birds had made their rookeries. These places are
infested with snakes which get a fat living from
eggs and young birds. The natives, however
seem to have a special sense for locating them. ’
* * #
The party dispersed—the islanders “yank-
ing ” the young birds out with the facility acquired
by long practice. JDavis started to do likewise,
enjoying the fun. He began bis attack along the
base of a low sandy bank, from the top of which
long tussocky g ass hung over in a fringe. The
day being warm he was clad only in pants and
a singlet open from the neck downwards.
Stooping to insert his hand into a hole, he was
startled by the sudden appearance of a snake,
which, aroused from a siesta in the sun, slipped
off the tussocks aod darted into the bosom of
his singlet ere he had time to move—its rough
body brushing humbly against his bare skin.
* * #
He had straightened himself up and man-
aged to keep his head —restraining a mad influ-
ence which at first prompted him to run. With-
out any unnecessary movement of muscles he
called to the nearest of his companions, who there-
upon, by way of pleasantry, hove a bird at him,
which he did not attempt to dodge, but received
plump on the chest. “No d d fooling,” said
Davis, quietly, “I want you here.” The reptile
kept quiet; doubtless it apprecia ed its quarters.
* * #
How to get rid of it was the query. One
or two ways suggested thenvelves—but they were
risky. By this time another of the party had
joined them with his counsels. They oecided on
a plan. One unbuckled Davis’s belt and gently
unfastened his pants. The other stood ready. At
a signal the first man pulled the t ousers down—
the other pulled the singlet up. Davis made a
spring that would not have disgraced an old man
kangaroo. The snake came out as quickly as he
had entered, and was promptly dealt with. The
tension over, Davis cursed the calling for a quarter
of an hour.
He’s never been “ birding ” since !
Hobart.
TAHUNE LINAH.
The Game of Spoof.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Scene—Private bar in a Southern city. Time—
-1 a.m. At the counter several well-known people,
mostly in evening dress and all more or less
“jolly,” are trying to make fun—the fun of a bar
in the small hours.
Enter a tall, healthy-looking, well-built man of
some 45 years and gentlemanly appearance, but
evidently under the ’fluence. He is invited to
drink, and doesn’t say no. Someone casually re-
marks that it is time all were in bed, and another
someone adds that “ it doesn’t take him long to get
to bed when once he starts.” After a time and by
very gradual methods the last-comer is drawn
into a heated argument as to how quickly it is
really possible to undress. The stranger declares
he can do so in one minute, and is promptly called
a liar. There is a great row. Peacemakers inter-
fere and urge that money shall bo staked. A
wager of <£s is made. It takes only a few more
inflaming remarks and the stranger agrees to
prove his assertion then and there. The barmaid
is induced to retire, which she apparently does.
A stakeholder and timekeeper are chosen, and the
word is given. Wildly excited, the victim begins.
First he cuts the laces of his boots from bottom to
top. Heedless of buttons and expense he tears
off his clothes and eventually stands stark naked
before the company. He has taken 64 seconds and
lost his bet. Conspirators pat him consolingly
on the back and cheeks with purposely soot-
blacked hands, and sympathetic doubts being ex-
pressed as to the accuracy of the time, the winner
declines to take his money. Taking advantage
of the discussion some of the more mischievous
play tricks with the dupe’s clothing, tying the
sleeves of his vest, and the legs of his pants into
knots, hiding his socks, and pouring soda into his
boots. All the while the poor fellow stands nude,
and, in re-dre-sing, discards his underwear, socks
included, rather than trouble to disentangle them.
Finally, part-dressed, he leaves the heated bat-
on a chilly night and wanders home with half his
wardrobe over his arm. He dies of acute pneu-
monia, two weeks later.
This is Spoof!
Melb.

A Melb woman drowned herself recently
in El wood swamp because she couldn’t pay an out-
standing bill of <£lo. The creditor told her not to
worry about it, but the debt weighed on her illogi-
cal mind till she couldn’t live any longer. If all
Melbourne people were built on the same plan it
would be impossible to drop into the Yarra at any
hour of the day or night without landing on the
bloated corpse of some representative citizen of
high standing; and there would be a dead land-
boomer in every tank of the great smellsorne city
and its suburbs : and every time a pump refused
to work, people would remark casually that there
was another conscience-stricken banker in the
well.
Send to your friends in England, America
and the Cape The Bulletin’s history of the Dean
case. Price, 6d, ; posted, 7d.
The trustees of Melb. National Gallery
bought a few pictures from the Vic. Artists’ Exhi-
bition the other week. They didn’t make the best
possible selection (not, at least, in the opinion of
anybody but themselves), yet some of the pictures
they did buy strike one as being far preferable to
the mannered, pretentious works which local art-
critics would fain have rushed them into admiring.
Art-criticism in the Melb. dailies is a worship of
the unpleasing and unnatural —a glorification of
crude eccentricity and cheap conceit. When an
art-jargonist condescends to praise an unpeculiar
bit of painting that only aspires to represent the
actual, he is careful to heap his eulogy upon an
undeserving object. The Melb. Gallery trustees
bought one very weak picture of the kind which
all the world can judge. But the newspapers had
praised this obviously bad imitation of a simple
fact.
Evolution*
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A day is coming on us when we won’t know what
it’s like
To walk, or run, or do a thing except to ride the
bike:
When, our evolution ended, we shall find our
legs have gone,
Except so much as will suffice to push the pedals
on.
For we all of us are riders—burglar, burgled man,
police,
Lawyer, client, surgeon, patient, Premier, Justice
of the Peace,
Man and woman, judge and jury, priest and peni-
tent alike,
Have given up all other things and taken to the
bike.
And if Heaven is a country where we’re given
what we love,
Jhe bicycle must follow to those realms of bliss
above;
And when we’ve travelled upwards, it will hardly
make us smile
To see Abraham or Isaac breaking records for the
mile.
A pair of stars will help to make a heavenly
machine,
Held close in glittering splendour by a comet fixed
between ;
And the angels and the patriarchs, and saints
and all the rest,
Will ride about for ever in the mansions of the
blest.
And the souls that pass away from Earth will see
on getting there,
St. Peter first among the twelve with half a length
to spare,
And will gaze with silent wonder at the recon-
structed figure
Of a cherub sitting strad-legs on a bright and
shining jigger.

25 miles during the night, and was consequently
many hours late. How did it happen ?

No mercy for the woman Williams could
be expected from the Victorian Government and

Borbhwick, the Morning Post man, has
already fultilled The Bulletin's prediction that
he would get a coronet from his grateful sovereign.
He is now a baron or an earl. One of these days
his lamentable newspaper will render a great ser-
vice to the Hempire by officially contradicting
some rumors concerning the health of one of
Albert Edward’s grandchildren, and then Borth-
wick will be raised to a dukery.
A Chinaman disappeared in Maoriland, and
suicide was suspected. The other day a settler
found a body in the river, undoubtedly that of
the Chow, and as the hands and feet were tied
together, and a huge stone affixed to the neck,
foul play was concluded. Here was a chance for
the local “cop!” At the inquest deceased’s
brother was called to identify, but : “ Him likee
Chinaman, but him no looker like my bludder,”
said the pagan. Then the intelligent jury decided
to view the body again and pull its clothes off.
The second examination revealed that there was
no body at all. A sheepskin had been used to
make the face, which was painted yellow ; pig-
bones to make the arms, and the entrails of some
animal, probably a sheep, had been utilised in
manufacturing the body. Likewise a set of goat’s
teeth gleamed in the mouth. The language used
by coroner and jury, in front of that policeman,
was remembersome.
Dear Bcjllexin,— Re prayers for rain,
many parsons point out that we have God’s own
assurance of his readiness to break up a drought
in response to pious supplication. Please note the
Bible “proofs ” —“And the Lord appeared to
him (King Solomon) by night and said : If I shut
up Heaven and there fall no rain, or if I give
orders and command the locust to devour the
land, or if I send pestilence among my people;
and my people being converted shall make suppli-
cation to me, and seek out my face, and do
penance for their most wicked ways; then shall I
hear from heaven, and willforgive their sins and
heal the land.” It seems to me that any scheme
of Christian church religion is an impudent fraud
unless it maintains the efficacy of prayers for rain,
all church religion being based on the Bible. If
the Bible be not inspired; if God didn’t give
orders and command some person or persons un-
known to write the aforequoted (from the second
book of Paralipomenon, verses 12 —l4) bang goes
the established faith. Any sort of belief in the
Bible is inconsistent with disbelief in the necessity
for rain-praying. And when rain falls on the top
of devotional exercises, as it did in N.S.W. lately,
no religious person can accept it for an answer to
prayer without taking the conversion of the people
for granted. How contemptible is the “rational”
preacher, so-called, who takes a text from the
Bible, as being in any sense the Word of God, and
yet is not prepared to swear to the conversion of
N.S.W. ! Your squatters and graziers and city
church-goers did penance for their wicked ways,
wherefore the rain fell—or else faith in Bible
inspiration is a lamentable error, a huge impedi-
ment in the way of truth. Let us take it that
the Bible is inspired from cover to cover. The
‘ ‘ rational ” parson’s suggestion that portions of the
Book are inspired, whilst other portions are plain
worldly balderdash, is not worth considering.
We’ll say the Bible is the Word of God, We will
congratulate the recent petitioners for rain on
their having proved the divinity of the book of
Paralipomenon, which, for its part, proves the
conversion of N.S.W. people. And now I want
to know why N.S.W. has omitted to fall upon its
knees and publicly thank God for the great
national conversion revealed by the late rainfall?
—Thorough.
